{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Do you worry too much? Stoicism can help","link":"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/stoicism-help-you-stop-worrying","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzY5NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMjY1MTEwNH0.mGpdbdm7VJF6_Tn3iUhynaB8CJm9Kw6bDDVcc--MmW0/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C413&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Stoicism is the philosophy that nothing about the world is good or bad in itself, and that we have control over both our judgments and our reactions to things. </li>\n <li>It is hardest to control our reactions to the things that come unexpectedly. </li>\n <li>By meditating every day on the \"worst case scenario,\" we can take the sting out of the worst that life can throw our way.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p><span></span>Are you a worrier? Do you imagine nightmare scenarios and then get worked up and anxious about them? Does your mind get caught in a horrible spiral of catastrophizing over even the smallest of things? Worrying, particularly imagining the worst case scenario, seems to be a natural part of being human and comes easily to a lot of us. It's awful, perhaps even dangerous, when we do it.</p>\n<p>But, there might just be an ancient wisdom that can help. It involves reframing this attitude for the better, and it comes from Stoicism. It's called \"premeditation,\" and it could be the most useful trick we can learn.</p>\n<h2>Practical Stoicism</h2>\n<p>Broadly speaking, Stoicism is the philosophy of choosing your judgments. Stoics believe that there is nothing about the universe that can be called good or bad, valuable or valueless, <em>in itself.</em> It's we who add these values to things. As Shakespeare's Hamlet says, \"There is nothing either good or bad, but thinking makes it so.\" Our minds color the things we encounter as being \"good\" or \"bad,\" and given that we control our minds, we therefore have control over all of our negative feelings.</p>\n<p>Put another way, Stoicism maintains that there's a gap between our experience of an event and our judgment of it. For instance, if someone calls you a smelly goat, you have an opportunity, however small and hard it might be, to pause and ask yourself, \"How will I judge this?\" What's more, you can even ask, \"How will I respond?\" We have power over which thoughts we entertain and the final say on our actions. Today, Stoicism has influenced and finds modern expression in the hugely effective \"cognitive behavioral therapy.\"</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"34b9bf12be3b451ed5f39ceb106d4b13\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzY5MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0OTgyNDI4N30.6U0r_3yhPx5UXexIFg_uL3ECa3qLqpa-3xwLrjmZRcM/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"5000\" id=\"727ca\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%207501%205000'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"7501\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Helping you practice Stoicism</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/goats-graze-on-a-hillside-as-part-of-fire-prevention-news-photo/1171383564?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Robyn Beck via Getty Images</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>One of the principal fathers of ancient Stoicism was the Roman statesmen, Seneca, who argued that the unexpected and unforeseen blows of life are the hardest to take control over. The shock of a misfortune can strip away the power we have to choose our reaction. For instance, being burglarized feels so horrible because we had felt so safe at home. A stomach ache, out of the blue, is harder than a stitch thirty minutes into a run. A sudden bang makes us jump, but a firework makes us smile. Fell swoops hurt more than known hardships.</p>\n<h2>What could possibly go wrong?</h2>\n<p>So, how can we resolve this? Seneca suggests a Stoic technique called \"premeditatio malorum\" or \"premeditation.\" At the start of every day, we ought to take time to indulge our anxious and catastrophizing mind. We should \"rehearse in the mind: exile, torture, war, shipwreck.\" We should meditate on the worst things that could happen: your partner will leave you, your boss will fire you, your house will burn down. Maybe, even, you'll die. </p>\n<p>This might sound depressing, but the important thing is that we do not stop there. </p>\n<blockquote>\n Stoicism has influenced and finds modern expression in the hugely effective \"cognitive behavioral therapy.\"\n</blockquote>\n<p>The Stoic also rehearses how they will <em>react</em> to these things as they come up. For instance, another Stoic (and Roman Emperor) Marcus Aurelius asks us to imagine all the mean, rude, selfish, and boorish people we'll come across today. Then, in our heads, we script how we'll respond when we meet them. We can shrug off their meanness, smile at their rudeness, and refuse to be \"implicated in what is degrading.\" Thus prepared, we take control again of our reactions and behavior.</p>\n<p>The Stoics cast themselves into the darkest and most desperate of conditions but then realize that they can and will endure. With premeditation, the Stoic is prepared and has the mental vigor necessary to take the blow on the chin and say, \"Yep, l can deal with this.\"</p>\n<h2>Catastrophizing as a method of mental inoculation</h2>\n<p>Seneca wrote: \"In times of peace, the soldier carries out maneuvers.\" This is also true of premeditation, which acts as the war room or training ground. The agonizing cut of the unexpected is blunted by preparedness. We can prepare the mind for whatever trials may come, in just the same way we can prepare the body for some endurance activity. The world can throw nothing as bad as that which our minds have already imagined. </p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>Stoicism teaches us to embrace our worrying mind but to embrace it as a kind of inoculation. With a frown over breakfast, try to spend five minutes of your day deliberately catastrophizing. Get your anti-anxiety battle plan ready and then face the world.</p>\n<p><em>Jonny Thomson teaches philosophy in Oxford. He runs a popular Instagram account called Mini Philosophy (@<a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/philosophyminis/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">philosophyminis</a>). His first book is <a href=\"https://www.amazon.co.uk/Mini-Philosophy-Small-Book-Ideas-ebook/dp/B08M3XDNPM/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Mini Philosophy: A Small Book of Big Ideas</a>.</em></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zTCT6cydr_4\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 16 Apr 2021 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzY5NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMjY1MTEwNH0.mGpdbdm7VJF6_Tn3iUhynaB8CJm9Kw6bDDVcc--MmW0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C413&height=700","linkMd5":"77a52d7db4868997594d3db64fe68b40","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn44@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-17-399_0a79091e8a122609.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":93126,"destBytes":93126,"author":"Jonny Thomson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzY5NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMjY1MTEwNH0.mGpdbdm7VJF6_Tn3iUhynaB8CJm9Kw6bDDVcc--MmW0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C413&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn44@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-17-399_0a79091e8a122609.webp","https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%207501%205000'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zTCT6cydr_4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn72@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-18-413_c0fad92319b62a62.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333841},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"World’s blackest black? Purdue made the world’s whitest white","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/worlds-blackest-black-purdue-made-the-worlds-whitest-white","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMjA3Njg1OX0.lvJQU60LhZ_7yvx1XMaZphMERV6h1yG0zPBZCrXDt7k/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Scientists at Purdue University announce the whitest white ever developed. It will be available as paint and a nanofilm.</li> \n <li>The new paint can actually cool surfaces on which it's applied, potentially reducing the need for climate-unfriendly air conditioners.</li> \n <li>This is the second whitest white to come from these researchers, and they believe this is about as white as any material could ever be.</li> \n</ul>\n<hr />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n</ul>\n<p> A few years ago, researchers announced the development of the blackest black ever, a place where colors go to die. It was called <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/robby-berman/what-we-see-when-we-look-at-something-outside-our-senses\" target=\"_self\">Vantablack</a>®, and it was so absorptive of visible light that only the tiniest amount escaped its surface to reflect back to our eyes. (All of that light energy is dissipated into the surrounding substrate, so Vantablack doesn't become hot.) </p>\n<p> In a new paper published in the journal <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1021/acsami.1c02368?_ga=2.237003968.1785032685.1618588483-1650361238.1618588483\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <em>ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</em></a>, scientists at Purdue University announced BaSO<sub>4</sub> (barium sulfate), the whitest white ever. BaSO<sub>4</sub> is practically impervious to the colors of the visible spectrum. Even better, while it's a very cool invention in the colloquial sense, it's also cool in the thermal sense. </p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n</ul>\n<h2>The coolest white</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"39ea36b37616efcaf4a9347c8520fc76\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzc4MC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NTY5NDg1Nn0.teMxYJ5N6yByb61vVyA8Qab89P2AjRNoFAhs40uctSg/img.png?width=980\" height=\"959\" id=\"d110a\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20959'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1440\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">The infrared image on the right shows how a square of the super-white paint and the board on which it's painted — shown in a normal image on the left — are cooler than the surrounding materials.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.purdue.edu/newsroom/releases/2021/Q2/the-whitest-paint-is-here-and-its-the-coolest.-literally..html\" target=\"_blank\">Purdue University</a>/Joseph Peoples</small> </p>\n<p> <br /> </p>\n<p> Most outside paints actually warm the surfaces to which they're applied. While there are already some reflective paints on the market, they only reflect 80 to 90 percent of sunlight, not enough for a cooling effect. </p>\n<p> By contrast, BaSO<sub>4</sub> results in 98.1 percent of sunlight bouncing off. <a href=\"https://www.purdue.edu/newsroom/releases/2021/Q2/the-whitest-paint-is-here-and-its-the-coolest.-literally..html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">According to</a> senior investigator <a href=\"https://engineering.purdue.edu/ME/People/ptProfile?resource_id=29343&amp;_ga=2.241871938.1785032685.1618588483-1650361238.1618588483\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Xuilin Ruan</a>, \"If you were to use this paint to cover a roof area of about 1,000 square feet, we estimate that you could get a cooling power of 10 kilowatts. That's more powerful than the central air conditioners used by most houses.\" </p>\n<p> Ruan and his colleagues tested BaSO<sub>4</sub> using <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermocouple\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">thermocouples</a>, high-accuracy devices that measure voltage to determine temperature. They found that at night, BaSO<sub>4</sub> surfaces are 19° F. cooler than surrounding air. Under strong sunlight the effect is not quite so extreme, but still dramatic: 8° of cooling. </p>\n<p> The researchers even found the paint works in cold weather. Testing it on a 43° F. day, the surface on which BaSO<sub>4</sub> was painted was a brisk 25° F. Their tests also indicate that BaSO<sub>4</sub> is hardy enough for outdoor conditions. </p>\n<h2>How the new white was developed</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d7cc78e8294503e0b2140f1a03ba8c4f\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzc4NS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NDg3NzQ2NH0.H2_jmUdsThgDOHRBYajWe1VbsuQ4cZcvYC-3IiyBPlk/img.png?width=980\" height=\"959\" id=\"8c81f\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20959'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1440\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Xuilin Ruan and a square of BaSO<sub>4</sub></small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.purdue.edu/newsroom/releases/2021/Q2/the-whitest-paint-is-here-and-its-the-coolest.-literally..html\" target=\"_blank\">Purdue University</a>/Jared Pike</small> </p>\n<p> <br /> </p>\n<p> Research in the field of radiative paint for cooling goes back to the 1970s, though Ruan's team has been working toward BaSO<sub>4</sub> for only six years. Along the way, they analyzed over 100 reflective materials, trying them out in about 50 experimental formulations. </p>\n<p> Lead author, postdoc Xiangyu Li explains, \"We looked at various commercial products, basically anything that's white. We found that using barium sulfate, you can theoretically make things really, really reflective, which means that they're really, really white.\" </p>\n<p> The whitest white paint before — developed by the same team just last autumn — depended on calcium carbonate, a compound commonly found in seashells, rocks, and blackboard chalk. </p>\n<p> The team crammed as many tiny BaSO<sub>4</sub> particles into the paint as possible. Says Li: \"Although a higher particle concentration is better for making something white, you can't increase the concentration too much. The higher the concentration, the easier it is for the paint to break or peel off.\" </p>\n<p> Another factor that makes the team's BaSO<sub>4</sub> formulation so reflective is that the researchers used barium sulfate particles of many different sizes. When it comes to reflecting light, size matters. </p>\n<p> Co-author and PhD student Joseph Peoples said, \"A high concentration of particles that are also different sizes gives the paint the broadest spectral scattering, which contributes to the highest reflectance.\" </p>\n<p> The team's formulation method, they report, is compatible with commercial paint production. </p>\n<h2>Cool support for the planet</h2>\n<p> Purdue has applied for patents relating to BaSO<sub>4</sub>, though there are as yet no plans to make it commercially available. </p>\n<p> However, the sooner they release it, the better. Air conditioning currently accounts for <a href=\"https://www.eia.gov/todayinenergy/detail.php?id=36692\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">12% of U.S. energy consumption</a>. Also, many air conditioners use hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs). While HFCs constitute just a small percentage of greenhouse gases, they trap thousands of times the amount of heat as carbon dioxide. </p>\n<p> Therefore, BaSO<sub>4</sub> can play a role in combating global warming by reducing energy consumption and the emission of HFCs. </p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/l6GCScFuqsY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMjA3Njg1OX0.lvJQU60LhZ_7yvx1XMaZphMERV6h1yG0zPBZCrXDt7k/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"1014e83d4555e2cbb038c72bfa23e07f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn45@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-17-451_56cdeb3423667d36.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":98474,"destBytes":98474,"author":"Robby Berman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMjA3Njg1OX0.lvJQU60LhZ_7yvx1XMaZphMERV6h1yG0zPBZCrXDt7k/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn45@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-17-451_56cdeb3423667d36.webp","https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20959'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/l6GCScFuqsY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn55@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-18-391_41baf626e959dca3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333841},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Why Aristotle didn't invent modern science","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/aristotle-didnt-invent-modern-science","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzM3Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MDA1NzY4MX0.p-Fs0fXyueyc5w9oft7z3OFz9tQi4XaTGf46a0XbvkE/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C132%2C0%2C132&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Modern science requires scrutinizing the tiniest of details and an almost irrational dedication to empirical observation. </li>\n <li>Many scientists believe that theories should be \"beautiful,\" but such argumentation is forbidden in modern science. </li>\n <li>Neglecting beauty would be a step too far for Aristotle.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p><span></span>Modern science has done astounding things: sending probes to Pluto, discerning the nature of light, vaccinating the globe. Its power to plumb the world's inner workings, many scientists and philosophers of science would say, hinges on its exacting attention to empirical evidence. The ethos guiding scientific inquiry might be formulated so: \"Credit must be given to theories only if what they affirm agrees with the observed facts.\"</p>\n<p>Those are the words of the Greek philosopher Aristotle, writing in the fourth century BCE. Why, then, was it only during the Scientific Revolution of the 17th century, two thousand years later, that science came into its own? Why wasn't it Aristotle who invented modern science?</p>\n<p>The answer is, first, that modern science attends to a different kind of observable fact than the sort that guided Aristotle. Second, modern science attends with an intensity — indeed an unreasonable narrow-mindedness — that Aristotle would have found to be more than a little unhinged. Let's explore those two ideas in turn.</p>\n<h2>Excruciating minutiae</h2>\n<p>In 1915, Albert Einstein proposed a new theory of gravitation — the general theory of relativity. It told a story radically different from the prevailing Newtonian theory; gravity, according to Einstein, was not a force but rather the manifestation of matter's propensity to travel along the straightest possible path through twisted spacetime. Relativity revised the notion of gravitation on the grandest conceptual scale, but to test it required the scrutiny of minutiae.</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img alt=\"science einstein relativity eddington\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"3b9290dca8dbd8c834b58f7e081df0cf\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzI0OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MTUwODU4M30.A-voduUpV6QIJWTbw6iqMtaPxYsT9t3MsXrLcIwL6x4/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"1849\" id=\"7127e\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201915%201849'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1915\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Einstein's general relativity predicts gravitational lensing.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Introduction_to_general_relativity#/media/File:Einstein_cross.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: NASA, ESA, and STScI / Public Domain via Wikipedia</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>When Arthur Eddington sought experimental evidence for the theory by measuring gravity's propensity to bend starlight, he photographed the same star field both in the night sky and then in close proximity to the eclipsed sun, looking for a slight displacement in the positions of the stars that would reveal the degree to which the sun's mass deflected their light. The change in position was on the order of a fraction of a millimeter on his photographic plates. In that minuscule discrepancy lay the reason to accept a wholly new vision of the nature of the forces that shape galaxies.</p>\n<p>Aristotle would not have thought to look in these places, at these diminutive magnitudes. Even the pre-scientific thinkers who believed that the behavior of things was determined by their microscopic structure did not believe it was possible for humans to discern that structure. When they sought a match between their ideas and the observed facts, they meant the facts that any person might readily encounter in the world around them: the gross motions of cannonballs and comets; the overall attunement of animals and their environs; the tastes, smells, and sounds that force themselves on our sensibilities without asking our permission. They were looking in the wrong place. The clues to the deepest truths have turned out to be deeply hidden.</p>\n<blockquote>\n Modern science attends with an intensity — indeed an unreasonable narrow-mindedness — that Aristotle would have found to be more than a little unhinged.\n</blockquote>\n<p>Even in those cases where the telling evidence is visible to the unassisted eye, the effort required to gather what's needed can be monumental. Charles Darwin spent nearly five years sailing around the world on a 90-foot-long ship, the <em>Beagle</em>, recording the sights and sounds that would prompt his theory of evolution by natural selection. Following his famous footsteps, the Princeton biologists Rosemary and Peter Grant have spent nearly 50 years visiting the tiny Galápagos island of Daphne Major every summer observing the local finch populations. In so doing, they witnessed the creation of a new species.</p>\n<p>Similarly excruciating demands are made by many other scientific projects, each consumed with the hunt for subtle detail. The LIGO experiment to measure gravitational waves commenced in the 1970s, was nearly closed down in the 1980s, began operating its detectors only in 2002, and then for well over a decade found nothing. Upgraded machinery revealed the waves at last in 2015. The scientists who had spent their entire careers working on LIGO were by then retired from their long-time university positions.</p>\n<h2>The \"iron rule\" of modern science</h2>\n<p>What pushes scientists to undertake these titanic efforts? That question brings me to the second way in which modern science's attitude to evidence differs from Aristotle's. There is something about the institutions of science, as the philosopher and historian Thomas Kuhn wrote, that \"forces scientists to investigate some part of nature in a detail and depth that would otherwise be unimaginable\". That something is an \"iron rule\" to the effect that, when publishing arguments for or against a hypothesis, <em>only empirical evidence counts</em>. That is to say, the only kind of argument that is allowed in science's official organs of communication is one that assesses a theory according to its ability to predict or explain the observable facts.</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"da0c89210feed89a088cf82c1be6197f\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzI3Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NzQ1OTExMX0.3NhsxtX9h9Om3zEJSVe7rsC_818DsCZAYPE4XUYs7Yc/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"696\" id=\"084f5\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20870%20696'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"870\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Aristotle and Alexander the Great</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle#/media/File:Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Charles Laplante / Public Domain via Wikipedia</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>Aristotle said that evidence counts, but he did not say that <em>only</em> evidence counts. To get a feel for the significance of this additional word, one of modern science's most significant ingredients, let me return to Eddington's attempt to test Einstein's theory by photographing stars during a solar eclipse.</p>\n<p>Eddington was himself as much of a theoretical as an experimental physicist. He was struck by the mathematical beauty of Einstein's theory, which he took as a sign of its superiority to the old, Newtonian physics. He might have devoted himself to promoting relativity theory on these grounds, proselytizing its aesthetic merits with his elegant writing style and his many scientific connections. But in scientific argument, only empirical evidence counts. To appeal to a theory's beauty is to transgress this iron rule.</p>\n<p>If Eddington was to advocate for Einstein, he would have to do so with measurements. Consequently, he found himself on a months-long expedition to Africa, where he and his collaborators sweated over their equipment day after day while praying for clear skies. In short, the iron rule forced Eddington to put beauty aside and to get on the boat. That is how scientists are pushed to hunt down the fine-grained, often elusive observations that endow science with its extraordinary power.</p>\n<h2>Irrational but effective</h2>\n<p>Though it may be a resounding success, there is something very peculiar about the iron rule. For Eddington and many other physicists, beauty is an important, even a crucial, consideration in determining the truth: \"We would not accept any theory as final unless it were beautiful,\" wrote the Nobelist Steven Weinberg.</p>\n<p>At the same time, the iron rule stipulates that beauty may play no part in scientific argument, or at least, in official, written scientific argument. The rule tells scientists, then, to ignore what they take to be an immensely valuable criterion for assessing theories. That seems oddly, even irrationally, narrow-minded. It turns out, then, that science's knowledge-making prowess is owed in great part to a kind of deliberate blindness, an unreasonable insistence that inquirers into nature consider nothing but observed fact.</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p><em><a href=\"http://www.strevens.org/\" target=\"_blank\">Michael Strevens</a> writes about science, understanding, complexity, and the nature of thought, and teaches philosophy at New York University. His most recent book, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Knowledge-Machine-Irrationality-Created-Science/dp/1631491377\" target=\"_blank\">The Knowledge Machine</a> (Liveright, 2020), sets out to explain how science works so well and why it took so long to get it right.</em><br /><br /></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/pHulrC2WIAA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 15 Apr 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzM3Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MDA1NzY4MX0.p-Fs0fXyueyc5w9oft7z3OFz9tQi4XaTGf46a0XbvkE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C132%2C0%2C132&height=700","linkMd5":"ca46a1d889408e892bc6ace2185b0f27","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn16@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-490_570a1b4c8d86ce74.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":141424,"destBytes":141424,"author":"Michael Strevens","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzM3Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MDA1NzY4MX0.p-Fs0fXyueyc5w9oft7z3OFz9tQi4XaTGf46a0XbvkE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C132%2C0%2C132&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn16@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-490_570a1b4c8d86ce74.webp","https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201915%201849'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20870%20696'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/pHulrC2WIAA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn58@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-18-388_d0acce2f7f09b0d1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333838},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"A canvas of nonsense: how Dada reflects a world gone mad through art","link":"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/nonsense-dada-world-mad-art","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzEzNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTQwOTU1N30.ORVXFv_HBr85ZERNmYA7xd9lAMEi2Iv3yssZoGJK2zI/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C318%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Dada is a provocative and surreal art movement born out of the madness of World War I.</li>\n <li><span></span>Tzara, a key Dada theorist, says Dada seeks \"to confuse and upset, to shake and jolt\" people from their comfort zones.</li>\n <li>Dada, as all avant-garde art, faces a key problem in how to stay true to its philosophy.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p><span></span>In a world gone mad, what can the few sane people left do? What can someone say when there are no words that seem up to the job? How can anyone hope to express ideas so terrible when doing so will only reduce those ideas?</p>\n<p>These are some of the things that inspired the Dada movement, and in its absurd, surreal, and chaotic nonsense, we find the voice of the voiceless.</p>\n<h2>The origin of Dadaism</h2>\n<p>Dada was a response to the madness of World War I. Reasonable, intelligent, and sensitive people looked at the blood and mud graveyards of the trenches and wondered how any meaning or goodness could ever be found again. How can someone make sense of a world where millions of young, happy, hopeful men were scythed down in a spray of bullets? How could life go back to normal when returning soldiers, blinded and disfigured from gas, lay homeless in the streets? Out of this awful revulsion, there came one bitter voice, and it said: \"Everything is nonsense.\"</p>\n<blockquote>\n Dada is the art of the nihilist. It smashes accepted wisdom, challenges norms and values, and offends, upsets, and provokes us to re-examine everything.\n</blockquote>\n<p>And so, the Dada movement expressed itself in absurdity. Tzara, the closest you get to a Dadaist philosopher, put it like this: \"Like everything in life, Dada is useless. Dada is without pretension, as life should be.\" Dada rejects all systems, all philosophy, all definite answers, and all truth. It is the living embrace of contradictions and nonsense. It seeks \"to confuse and upset people, to shake and jolt\". It aims to shout down the \"shamefaced sex of comfortable compromise and good manners,\" when actually \"everything happens in a completely idiotic way.\"</p>\n<p>In short, Dada is a response to the world when all the usual methods have broken down. It's the recognition that dinner party conversations, Hollywood blockbusters, and Silicon Valley are not how life actually is. This is a false reality and order, like some kind of veneer. </p>\n<p>The Dada response to life is to embrace the personal and passionate madness of it all, where \"the intensity of a personality is transposed directly, clearly into the work.\" It's to recognize the unique position of an artist, who can convey ideas and feelings in a way that goes beyond normal understanding. Art goes straight to the soul, but the intensity of it all can be hard to \"enjoy\" in the strictest sense.</p>\n<h2>Where is this Dada?</h2>\n<p>For instance, Dada is seen in the poems of Hugo Ball who wrote in meaningless <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=z_8Wg40F3yo&amp;ab_channel=Mr.DSCH\" target=\"_blank\">foreign-sounding words</a>. It's in Hausmann, who wrote works in <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sat0LIaOVTM&amp;ab_channel=MorsMea\" target=\"_blank\">disconnected phonemes</a>. It's found in Duchamp's iconoclastic \"<a href=\"https://www.tate.org.uk/art/artworks/duchamp-fountain-t07573\" target=\"_blank\">Fountain</a>\" that sought to question what art or an artist really meant. It's in Hans Richter's short film \"<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JJoBf_pNdt4\" target=\"_blank\">Ghost before Breakfast</a>,\" which has an incoherent montage of images, loosely connected by the theme of inanimate objects in revolt. And, it's in <a href=\"https://www.tate.org.uk/art/artists/kurt-schwitters-1912\" target=\"_blank\">Kurt Schwitters'</a> \"psychological collages\" which present fragments of objects, juxtaposed together.</p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img alt=\"kurt schwitters dada dadaism\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"5cb88dbd582162a6b4cf9dc4535e8762\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzE0Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyODMxNTkzOH0.mZfOUvicQY4dtolxygvFD1VoJNwbRX0x7XCwb6Oi7RM/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"671\" id=\"c3f92\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20578%20671'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"578\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Kurt Schwitters, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merz_(art_style)\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"Merz (art style)\">Merz</a>-drawing 85, Zig-Zag Red, 1920, collage</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurt_Schwitters#/media/File:MERZZEICHNUNG_85_ZICKZACKROT_(MERZDRAWING_85_ZIG-ZAG_RED).PNG\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Kurt Schwitters / Public Domain via Wikipedia</small></p>\n<p>Dada is intended to shock. It's an artistic jolt asking, or <em>demanding</em>, that the viewers reorient themselves in some way. It is designed to make us feel uncomfortable and does not make for easy appreciation. It's only when we're thrown so drastically outside of our comfort zone in this way that Dada asks us to question how things are. It shakes us out of a conformist stupor to look afresh at things.<br /></p>\n<h2>The paradox of Dadaism</h2>\n<p>Of course, like all avant-garde art, Dada needs to address one major problem: how do you <em>stay </em>so provocative, so radical, and so anti-establishment when you also seek success? How can maverick rebels stay so as they get a mortgage and want a good school for their kids? The problem is that young, inventive, and idealistic artists are inevitably sucked into the world of profit and commodity. </p>\n<p>As Grayson Perry, a British modern artist,<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2017/may/27/grayson-perry-the-most-popular-art-exhibition-ever\" target=\"_blank\"> wrote</a>: \"What starts as a creative revolt soon becomes co-opted as the latest way to make money,\" and what was once fresh and challenging \"falls away to reveal a predatory capitalist robot.\" With Dada, how long can someone actually <em>live</em> in a world of nonsense and nihilistic absurdity?</p>\n<p>But there will always be new blood to keep movements like Dada going. As the revolutionaries of yesterday become the rich mansion-owners of today, there will be hot, young things to come and take up the mantle. There will always be something to challenge and questions to be asked. So, art movements like Dada will always be in the vanguard.</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>Dada is the art of the nihilist. It smashes accepted wisdom, challenges norms and values, and offends, upsets, and provokes us to re-examine everything. It's an absurd art form that reflects the reality it perceives — that life is nothing more than a dissonant patchwork of egos floating in an abyss of nothing.</p>\n<p><em>Jonny Thomson teaches philosophy in Oxford. He runs a popular Instagram account called Mini Philosophy (@<a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/philosophyminis/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">philosophyminis</a>). His first book is <a href=\"https://www.amazon.co.uk/Mini-Philosophy-Small-Book-Ideas-ebook/dp/B08M3XDNPM/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Mini Philosophy: A Small Book of Big Ideas</a>.</em></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/AFo4fQHS-Vw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 21 Apr 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzEzNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTQwOTU1N30.ORVXFv_HBr85ZERNmYA7xd9lAMEi2Iv3yssZoGJK2zI/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C318%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"503d4ff3fcf9e0217613b26b6ed722cb","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn3@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-459_5750ac71237a7406.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":68122,"destBytes":68122,"author":"Jonny Thomson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzEzNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTQwOTU1N30.ORVXFv_HBr85ZERNmYA7xd9lAMEi2Iv3yssZoGJK2zI/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C318%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn3@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-459_5750ac71237a7406.webp","https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20578%20671'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/AFo4fQHS-Vw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn59@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-18-393_710c133fd94151ee.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333842},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"‘Mad honey’: The rare hallucinogen from the mountains of Nepal","link":"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/mad-honey","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA5MTM1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzQ4MDc4MX0.W5lOy-ZlnGdgIuQSOK4U0nHOzY1rms10yBZMHZuoNJU/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C83%2C0%2C83&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Mad honey is produced by bees who feed on specific species of rhododendron plants, which grow in mountainous regions like those surrounding the Black Sea.</li> \n <li>People have used mad honey for centuries for recreational, medicinal, and military purposes. Low doses cause euphoria and lightheadedness, while high doses cause hallucinations and, in rare cases, death.</li> \n <li>Mad honey is still harvested and sold today, though it's illegal in some nations.</li> \n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p> On the mountainsides of Nepal and Turkey, bees produce a strange and dangerous concoction: mad honey. </p>\n<p> It's a rare variety of the natural fluid. Compared to the several hundred other types of honey produced around the world, mad honey is redder and slightly more bitter tasting, and it comes from the world's largest honey bee, <em>Apis dorsata laboriosa</em>. </p>\n<p> <strong>Mad about honey</strong> </p>\n<p> But what really distinguishes mad honey are its physiological effects. In lower doses, mad honey causes dizziness, lightheadedness, and euphoria. Higher doses can <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/dmt-beings\" target=\"_self\">cause hallucinations</a>, vomiting, loss of consciousness, seizures, and, in rare cases, death. </p>\n<p> Here's one account of what it's like to take a moderate dose of mad honey, provided by a VICE producer who traveled to Nepal to join mad honey hunters on a harvesting expedition: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"I ate two teaspoons, the amount recommended by the honey hunters, and after about 15 minutes, I started to feel a high similar to weed,\" wrote David Caprara for <a href=\"https://www.vice.com/en/article/wdbz55/hunting-for-hallucinogenic-honey-in-nepal-v23n6\" target=\"_blank\">VICE</a>. </em></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><em> \"I felt like my body was cooling down, starting from the back of my head and down through my torso. A deep, icy hot feeling settled in my stomach and lasted for several hours. The honey was delicious, and though a few of the hunters passed out from eating a bit too much, no one suffered from the projectile vomiting or explosive diarrhea I'd been warned about.\"</em> </p>\n<p> Here's another account from Will Brendza at <a href=\"https://therooster.com/blog/the-mad-honey-experiment-nepalese-mad-honey-is-as-trippy-as-it-is-tasty-but-very-unlike-other-psychedelics\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">The Rooster</a>: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"Within 40ish minutes I could feel the honey creeping up on me. The back of my head started to tingle, like I was getting a scalp massage. Then, from within, I felt a warmth around my heart, in my chest and abdomen. Things slowed down a little, and my state of mind became tranquil. By the time we left the restaurant I was feeling good and strange.\" </em></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><em> \"There are no visuals, though. The high is very much a bodily one and a mental one; a warm and relaxed sensation more like a sedative than your conventional psychedelic.\"</em> </p>\n<p> <strong>What is mad honey?</strong> </p>\n<p> The psychoactive effects of mad honey stem not from bees but from what bees feed on in certain regions: a genus of flowering plants called rhododendrons. All species of these plants contain a group of neurotoxic compounds called grayanotoxins. When bees feed on the nectar and pollen of certain types of rhododendrons, the insects ingest grayanotoxins, which eventually make their way into the bees' honey, effectively making it \"mad.\" </p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img alt=\"Mad honey\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"3a1bf0aa1e7bd7cbc71ad1399b12a127\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA5MTM3MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyNzkzMDYxNn0.RqaK1Z6Mv1Iql27YEeAfqEgWSPbWiFegmQeOUaNSVpo/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"1440\" id=\"83ebd\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" width=\"1920\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Rhododendron ferrugineum</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhododendron#/media/File:Alpenroos.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">albert kok</a> </small> </p>\n<p> Bees are more likely to produce mad honey when and where rhododendrons are dominating. The reason has to do with scarcity: With fewer types of plants to feed on, the insects feed almost solely on rhododendrons, so they consume more grayanotoxins. The result is especially pure mad honey.<br /> </p>\n<p> But accessing honeycombs that contain mad honey can be difficult. One reason is that rhododendrons grow best in higher altitudes, and bees often build their hives on cliffs near the plants, meaning harvesters have to climb mountainsides to harvest the honey. </p>\n<p> However, harvesters bold enough to go for the honeycombs stand to profit. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2020/jan/16/creating-a-buzz-turkish-beekeepers-risk-life-and-limb-to-make-mad-honey\" target=\"_blank\"><em>The Guardian</em></a> reported that a kilogram of high-quality mad honey can sell for about $360 in shops around Turkey, while <a href=\"https://www.nationalgeographic.com/magazine/article/honey-hunters-bees-climbing-nepal\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>National Geographic</em></a> noted that a pound of mad honey goes for about $60 on <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/ideafeed/why-black-markets-thrive-in-developing-countries\" target=\"_self\">Asian black markets</a>. In general, the value of mad honey is much higher than regular honey. </p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-youtube\"> <span class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"5ecf988b2d6f7ef4359d97a9a231db0e\" style=\"display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;\"><iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"auto\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/wDOvmhqvIA8?rel=0\" style=\"position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;\" width=\"100%\"></iframe></span> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"> <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=wDOvmhqvIA8\" target=\"_blank\">www.youtube.com</a> </small> </p>\n<p> That's partly because many people believe mad honey has more medical value than regular honey. In the Black Sea region and beyond, people use it to treat conditions like hypertension, diabetes, arthritis, and <a href=\"https://www.nationalgeographic.com/magazine/article/honey-hunters-bees-climbing-nepal\" target=\"_blank\">sore throat</a>, though the research on the <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3404272/\" target=\"_blank\">medical benefits of hallucinogenic honey from Nepal and Turkey is unclear</a>.<br /> </p>\n<p> In northeastern Asia, some buyers believe <a href=\"https://www.vice.com/en/article/wdbz55/hunting-for-hallucinogenic-honey-in-nepal-v23n6\" target=\"_blank\">mad honey treats erectile dysfunction</a>, which might explain why the majority of cases of mad honey poisonings involve middle-aged men, as noted in a 2018 report published in the journal <a href=\"https://pubs.rsc.org/en/content/articlehtml/2018/ra/c8ra01924j#:~:text=However%2C%20mad%20honey%20intoxication%20cases,by%20Korean%20travelers%20in%20Nepal.\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>RSC Advances</em></a><em>.</em> </p>\n<p> <strong>How does mad honey affect the body?</strong> </p>\n<p> Although the medicinal benefits of mad honey aren't clear, what's certain is that humans can be <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3404272/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">poisoned by consuming too much grayanotoxin-rich honey</a>, which can cause dangerous decreases to blood pressure and heart rate. </p>\n<p> Forensic toxicologist Justin Brower elaborated on his blog, <a href=\"https://naturespoisons.com/2014/05/08/grayanotoxins-of-rhododendrons-and-mad-honey/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Nature's Poisons</em></a>: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"Grayanotoxins exert their toxicity by binding to sodium ion channels on cell membranes and preventing them from closing quickly, like aconitine. The result is a state of depolarization in which sodium ions are freely flowing into the cells, and calcium influx is on the rise.\"</em> </p>\n<p> This process can lead to a series of symptoms involving increased sweating, salivation, and nausea, Brower said, noting that symptoms typically disappear within 24 hours, as they did for a <a href=\"https://www.seattletimes.com/seattle-news/3-new-cases-of-foodborne-illness-identified-in-king-county/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">man in Seattle who suffered mad honey poisoning in 2011</a>. While the exact amount of mad honey it takes to become poisoned depends on the individual and the quality of the honey, the 2018 <em>RSC Advances </em>report noted: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"Consumption of about 15-30 g mad honey leads to intoxication, and symptoms appear after half to 4 [hours]. The level of intoxication not only depends on the amount of mad honey consumed but also on the grayanotoxin concentration in the honey and the season of production. According to <a href=\"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15496712/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Ozhan et al.</a>, consumption of one teaspoon of mad honey may lead to poisoning.\"</em> </p>\n<p> Although Turkey records about a dozen cases of mad honey poisonings per year, a 2012 study published in <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3404272/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Cardiovascular Toxicology</em></a> noted that it's rare for people to die from the substance, though cases of animal deaths have been reported. </p>\n<p> <strong>Mad honey throughout history</strong> </p>\n<p> The strange effects of mad honey have captivated people near the Black Sea for millennia. One of the oldest accounts comes from 401 BCE, when Greek soldiers were marching through the Turkish town of Trabzon and came across a bounty of mad honey. The Athenian military leader and philosopher Xenophon wrote in his book <a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?id=_9cwAQAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA119&amp;lpg=PA119&amp;dq=ground,+in+consequence,+in+great+numbers,+as+if+there+had+been+a+defeat;+and+there+was+general+dejection.+The+next+day+no+one+of+them+was+found+dead;+and+they+recovered+their+senses+about+the+same+hour+that+they+had+lost+them+on+the+preceding+day;+and+on+the+third+and+fourth+days+they+got+up+as+if+after+having+taken+physi&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=6Cp5IYPWb_&amp;sig=ACfU3U3aNR755pNxlIbimm3HTEf-WZiABw&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=2ahUKEwiz7cD2no7wAhXDGM0KHcbGBKcQ6AEwAnoECAEQAw#v=onepage&amp;q=ground%2C%20in%20consequence%2C%20in%20great%20numbers%2C%20as%20if%20there%20had%20been%20a%20defeat%3B%20and%20there%20was%20general%20dejection.%20The%20next%20day%20no%20one%20of%20them%20was%20found%20dead%3B%20and%20they%20recovered%20their%20senses%20about%20the%20same%20hour%20that%20they%20had%20lost%20them%20on%20the%20preceding%20day%3B%20and%20on%20the%20third%20and%20fourth%20days%20they%20got%20up%20as%20if%20after%20having%20taken%20physi&amp;f=false\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Anabasis</em></a>: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"The number of bee-hives was extraordinary, and all the soldiers that ate of the combs, lost their senses, vomited, and were affected with purging, and none of them were able to stand upright; such as had eaten only a little were like men greatly intoxicated, and such as had eaten much were like mad-men, and some like persons at the point of death.\" </em></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><em> \"They lay upon the ground, in consequence, in great numbers, as if there had been a defeat; and there was general dejection. The next day no one of them was found dead; and they recovered their senses about the same hour that they had lost them on the preceding day; and on the third and fourth days they got up as if after having taken physic.\"</em> </p>\n<p> Centuries later, in 67 BCE, Roman soldiers weren't so lucky. As the soldiers pursued King Mithridates of Pontus and his Persian army, they stumbled across mad honey that the Persians had intentionally left behind, intending to use the substance as a bioweapon. Vaughn Bryant, a professor of anthropology at Texas A&amp;M University, explained in a <a href=\"https://research.tamu.edu/2014/11/03/how-eating-mad-honey-cost-pompey-the-great-1000-soldiers/#:~:text=Mad%20honey%20originated%20in%20the,called%20grayanotoxin%20in%20their%20nectars.&amp;text=Near%20Trabzon%20(in%20Northeastern%20Turkey,stolen%20from%20some%20nearby%20beehives.\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">press release</a>: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"The Persians gathered pots full of local honey and left them for the Roman troops to find. They ate the honey, became disoriented and couldn't fight. The Persian army returned and killed over 1,000 Roman troops with few losses of their own.\"</em> </p>\n<p> But mad honey was more often used for nonviolent purposes. People in the Black Sea region have long consumed small amounts of the substance (about a teaspoon's worth), in boiling milk or on its own, both for pleasure and as a folk medicine. </p>\n<p> In the 18th century, merchants in the Black Sea region sold honey to the Europeans, who infused liquor with a bit of the substance to enjoy its milder effects. </p>\n<p> <strong>Mad honey today</strong> </p>\n<p> Today, beekeepers in Nepal and Turkey still harvest mad honey, though it represents a small fraction of the nations' total honey production. Both countries allow the production, sale, and exportation of mad honey, but the substance is illegal in other nations, like South Korea, which <a href=\"https://pubs.rsc.org/en/content/articlehtml/2018/ra/c8ra01924j\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">banned the substance in 2005</a>. </p>\n<p> While interested buyers in the U.S. can purchase mad honey from countries like Nepal and Turkey, it might be worth sticking with the regular stuff. After all, the handful of experiences posted on the website of the non-profit psychedelic research organization Erowid.org don't sound too enticing. </p>\n<p>One user <a href=\"https://erowid.org/experiences/exp.php?ID=114041\" target=\"_blank\">said</a> they \"wouldn't even recommend trying it.\" Another user suffered mad honey poisoning after taking too much, <a href=\"https://erowid.org/experiences/exp.php?ID=110677\" target=\"_blank\">writing</a> that the \"symptoms can seem life threatening\" and that they hope their report might help \"some poor bastard out there not make the same mistake.\"</p>\n<div> \n</div>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n</ul>\n<div> \n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/kQqJ0MGGx3E\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 23 Apr 2021 15:02:22 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA5MTM1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzQ4MDc4MX0.W5lOy-ZlnGdgIuQSOK4U0nHOzY1rms10yBZMHZuoNJU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C83%2C0%2C83&height=700","linkMd5":"f366b3a08552eaaa537a7fa2cb60aadd","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn23@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-470_023da3be38a6a4b1.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":203190,"destBytes":203190,"author":"Stephen Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA5MTM1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzQ4MDc4MX0.W5lOy-ZlnGdgIuQSOK4U0nHOzY1rms10yBZMHZuoNJU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C83%2C0%2C83&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn23@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-470_023da3be38a6a4b1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/kQqJ0MGGx3E":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn40@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-18-751_1e91c11392e61005.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333835},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"How gratitude makes you more attractive","link":"https://bigthink.com/john-templeton-foundation/attitude-of-gratitude","description":"<iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"100%\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.rebelmouse.com/res/scraper/embed/?jwplayer_video_url=https%3A%2F%2Fcontent.jwplatform.com%2Fplayers%2FREpy88Mi-FvQKszTI.js\" width=\"100%\"></iframe>\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>When someone says thank you, who is it for? According to Dr. Sara Algoe, expressions of gratitude have a positive effect on the person receiving the message, the person delivering it, and even those who witness the exchange. These types of social interactions are crucial for building lasting relationships with romantic partners, friends, and coworkers.</li>\n <li>\"When we say 'thank you,' we're sending a message to the person who just did something nice for us, that they are valued, that they're seen, that the thing that they did for us was worth doing in the first place,\" Algoe says. </li>\n <li>Expressing gratitude is easy, and the research shows that the benefits far outweigh the effort. </li>\n</ul>\n<div>\n <br />\n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/sVdaCsL5Q5o\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 15 Apr 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"87f4f14433884fab54b78b78fcb6619f","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Dr. Sara Algoe","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/sVdaCsL5Q5o":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn88@2020_1/2021/04/23/17-38-18-296_8d7b9b5033a8e2c0.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333838},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"New galaxy clusters found hiding in plain sight","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/new-galaxy-clusters","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4OTc3My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MTM0NDYxOH0.RXnlaf74XYTS6AKHvLkUQBFGLPNhUpjFIn9CLqONpog/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C11%2C0%2C11&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />MIT astronomers have discovered new and unusual galactic neighborhoods that previous studies overlooked.\n<hr />\n<p> Their <a href=\"https://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.3847/1538-4357/abe1bc\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">results</a>, published in March, suggest that roughly 1 percent of galaxy clusters look atypical and can be easily misidentified as a single bright galaxy. As researchers launch new cluster-hunting telescopes, they must heed these findings or risk having an incomplete picture of the universe.</p>\n<p>Galaxy clusters contain hundreds to thousands of galaxies bound together by gravity. They move through a hot soup of gas called the intracluster medium, which contains more mass than all the stars in all the galaxies within it. This hot gas fuels star formation as it cools and emits X-ray radiation that we can observe with space-based telescopes. </p>\n<p>This bright gas cloud creates a fuzzy halo of X-rays around galaxy clusters, making them stand out from more discrete point sources of X-rays produced by, for example, a star or quasar. However, some galactic neighborhoods break this mold, as MIT Associate Professor Michael McDonald learned nine years ago.</p>\n<p>In 2012, McDonald discovered a cluster unlike any other, which shone bright like a point source in the X-ray. Its central galaxy hosts a ravenous black hole that consumes matter and spews X-rays so bright as to drown out the diffuse radiation of the intracluster medium. In its core, the cluster forms stars at a rate roughly 500 times higher than most other clusters, giving it the blue glow of a young star population instead of the typical red hue of aging stars.</p>\n<p>\"We'd been looking for a system like this for decades,\" McDonald says of the Phoenix cluster. And yet, it had been observed and passed over years prior, assumed to be a single galaxy instead of a cluster. \"It'd been in the archive for decades and no one saw it. They were looking past it because it didn't look right.\"</p>\n<p>And so, McDonald wondered, what other unusual clusters might be lurking in the archive, waiting to be found? Thus, the Clusters Hiding in Plain Sight (CHiPS) survey was born.</p>\n<p>Taweewat Somboonpanyakul, a graduate student in McDonald's lab, devoted his entire PhD to the CHiPS survey. He began by selecting potential cluster candidates from decades of X-ray observations. He used existing data from ground-based telescopes in Hawaii and New Mexico, and visited the Magellan telescopes in Chile to take new images of the remaining sources, hunting for neighboring galaxies that would reveal a cluster. In the most promising cases, he zoomed in with higher-resolution telescopes such as the space-based Chandra X-Ray Observatory and Hubble Space Telescope.</p>\n<p>After six years, the CHiPS survey has now come to a close. Today in <em>The Astrophysical Journal</em>, Somboonpanyakul published the survey's <a href=\"https://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.3847/1538-4357/abe1bc\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">cumulative results</a>, which include the discovery of three new galaxy clusters. One of these clusters, CHIPS1911+4455, is similar to the rapidly-star-forming Phoenix cluster and was described in a <a href=\"https://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.3847/2041-8213/abd540\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">paper</a> in January in <em>The Astrophysical Journal Letters</em>. It's an exciting finding since astronomers know of just a few other Phoenix-like clusters. This cluster invites further study, however, as it has a twisted shape with two extended arms, whereas all other rapidly-cooling clusters are circular. The researchers believe it may have collided with a smaller galaxy cluster. \"It's super unique compared to all the galaxy clusters that we now know,\" says Somboonpanyakul.</p>\n<p>In all, the CHiPS survey revealed that older X-ray surveys missed roughly 1 percent of galactic neighborhoods because they look different than the typical cluster. This can have significant implications, since astronomers study galaxy clusters to learn about how the universe expands and evolves. \"We need to find all the clusters to get those things right,\" McDonald explains. \"Ninety-nine percent completion isn't enough if you want to push the frontier.\"</p>\n<p>As scientists discover and study more of these unusual galaxy clusters, they may better understand how they fit into the broader cosmic picture. At this point, they don't know whether a small number of clusters are always in this strange, Phoenix-like state, or if this is perhaps a typical phase that all clusters undergo for a short period of time — roughly 20 million years, a fleeting moment by spacetime standards. It's difficult for astronomers to tell the difference, as they only get a single snapshot of each cluster nearly frozen in time. But with more data, they can make better models of the physics governing these galactic neighborhoods.</p>\n<p>The conclusion of the CHiPS survey coincides with the launch of a new X-ray telescope, eROSITA, which aims to grow our catalogue of clusters from a few hundred to tens of thousands. But unless they change the way they look for those clusters, they will miss hundreds that deviate from the norm. \"The people that are building out the cluster searches for this new X-ray telescope need to be aware of this work,\" says McDonald. \"If you miss 1 percent of the clusters, there's a fundamental limit to how well you can understand the universe.\"</p>\n<p>This research was supported, in part, by the Kavli Research Investment Fund at MIT, and by NASA through the Guest Observer programs for the Chandra X-ray Observatory and Hubble Space Telescope.</p>\n<p>Reprinted with permission of <a href=\"http://news.mit.edu/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">MIT News</a>. Read the <a href=\"https://news.mit.edu/2021/mit-astronomers-discover-new-galaxy-clusters-hiding-plain-sight-0326\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">original article</a>.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zUbYGFjfZMw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 23 Apr 2021 06:43:45 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4OTc3My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MTM0NDYxOH0.RXnlaf74XYTS6AKHvLkUQBFGLPNhUpjFIn9CLqONpog/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C11%2C0%2C11&height=700","linkMd5":"dfde5db7c8ed00a63119c6721ee4addf","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn98@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-350_19d0e3382a3da126.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":13158,"destBytes":13158,"author":"Kelso Harper | MIT Kavli Institute","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4OTc3My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MTM0NDYxOH0.RXnlaf74XYTS6AKHvLkUQBFGLPNhUpjFIn9CLqONpog/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C11%2C0%2C11&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn98@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-350_19d0e3382a3da126.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zUbYGFjfZMw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn98@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-18-398_d4610f746aa7986a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333840},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"What the rise of digital nomads can tell us about the next wave of remote working","link":"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/remote-working-digital-nomads","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzk4OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMjY3MTQ1Nn0.aR_ROcBjLvtc-EcW-aBEFehwmVkSomehqbVFDpiAQn0/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C60%2C0%2C299&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<p>If one thing is clear about remote work, it's this: <a href=\"https://www.pwc.com/us/en/library/covid-19/us-remote-work-survey.html\" target=\"_blank\">Many people prefer it and don't want their bosses to take it away</a>. </p>\n<hr />\n<p>When the pandemic forced office employees into lockdown and cut them off from spending in-person time with their colleagues, they almost immediately realized that they favor remote work over their traditional office routines and norms. </p>\n<p>As remote workers of all ages contemplate their futures – <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2021/03/26/981817613/microsoft-facebook-uber-plan-to-reopen-offices\" target=\"_blank\">and as some offices and schools start to reopen</a> – <a href=\"https://www.usatoday.com/story/money/2021/01/05/jobs-home-29-professionals-would-quit-if-forced-go-back-office/4142830001/\" target=\"_blank\">many Americans are asking hard questions about whether they wish to return to their old lives</a>, and what they're willing to sacrifice or endure in the years to come.</p>\n<p>Even before the pandemic, there were people asking whether office life jibed with their aspirations.</p>\n<p>We spent years studying “<a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1007/s40558-020-00177-z\" target=\"_blank\">digital nomads</a>\" – workers who had left behind their homes, cities and most of their possessions to embark on what they call “location independent\" lives. <a href=\"https://global.oup.com/academic/product/digital-nomads-9780190931780?cc=us&amp;lang=en&amp;#\" target=\"_blank\">Our research</a> taught us several important lessons about the conditions that push workers away from offices and major metropolitan areas, pulling them toward new lifestyles.</p>\n<p>Legions of people now have the chance to reinvent their relationship to their work in much the same way.</p>\n<h2>Big-city bait and switch</h2>\n<p>Most digital nomads started out excited to work in career-track jobs for prestigious employers. Moving to cities like New York and London, they wanted to spend their free time meeting new people, going to museums and trying out new restaurants.</p>\n<p>But then came the burnout. </p>\n<p>Although these cities certainly host institutions that can inspire creativity and cultivate new relationships, digital nomads rarely had time to take advantage of them. Instead, <a href=\"https://www.coli.org/quarter-1-2019-cost-of-living-index-released-3-2/\" target=\"_blank\">high cost of living</a>, time constraints and work demands contributed to an oppressive culture of materialism and workaholism.</p>\n<p>Pauline, 28, who worked in advertising helping large corporate clients to develop brand identities through music, likened city life for professionals in her peer group to a “hamster wheel.\" (The names used in this article are pseudonyms, as required by research protocol.)</p>\n<p>“The thing about New York is it's kind of like the battle of the busiest,\" she said. “It's like, 'Oh, you're so busy? No, I'm so busy.'\"</p>\n<p>Most of the digital nomads we studied had been lured into what urbanist <a href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60042.The_Rise_of_the_Creative_Class\" target=\"_blank\">Richard Florida termed “creative class\" jobs</a> – positions in design, tech, marketing and entertainment. They assumed this work would prove fulfilling enough to offset what they sacrificed in terms of time spent on social and creative pursuits. </p>\n<p>Yet these digital nomads told us that their jobs were far less interesting and creative than they had been led to expect. Worse, their employers continued to demand that they be “all in\" for work – and accept the controlling aspects of office life without providing the development, mentorship or meaningful work they felt they had been promised. As they looked to the future, they saw only more of the same. </p>\n<p>Ellie, 33, a former business journalist who is now a freelance writer and entrepreneur, told us: “A lot of people don't have positive role models at work, so then it's sort of like 'Why am I climbing the ladder to try and get this job? This doesn't seem like a good way to spend the next twenty years.'\"</p>\n<p>By their late 20s to early 30s, digital nomads were actively researching ways to leave their career-track jobs in top-tier global cities. </p>\n<h2>Looking for a fresh start</h2>\n<p>Although they left some of the world's most glamorous cities, the digital nomads we studied were not homesteaders working from the wilderness; they needed access to the conveniences of contemporary life in order to be productive. Looking abroad, they quickly learned that places like Bali in Indonesia, and Chiang Mai in Thailand had the necessary infrastructure to support them at a fraction of the cost of their former lives. </p>\n<p>With more and more companies now offering employees the choice to work <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2020/06/22/870029658/get-a-comfortable-chair-permanent-work-from-home-is-coming\" target=\"_blank\">remotely</a>, there's no reason to think digital nomads have to travel to southeast Asia – or even leave the United States – to transform their work lives.</p>\n<p>During the pandemic, some people have already <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-remote-work-is-reshaping-americas-urban-geography-11614960100\" target=\"_blank\">migrated away from the nation's most expensive real estate markets</a> to <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2020/11/16/931400786/small-cities-are-a-big-draw-for-remote-workers-during-the-pandemic\">smaller cities and towns</a> to be closer to nature or family. Many of these places still possess vibrant local cultures. As commutes to work disappear from daily life, such moves could leave remote workers with more available income and more free time.</p>\n<p>The digital nomads we studied often used savings in time and money to try new things, like exploring side hustles. <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.5465/amj.2018.0164\" target=\"_blank\">One recent study</a> even found, somewhat paradoxically, that the sense of empowerment that came from embarking on a side hustle actually improved performance in workers' primary jobs. </p>\n<p>The future of work, while not entirely remote, will undoubtedly offer more remote options to many more workers. Although <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2020/10/14/923428794/from-the-folks-who-brought-you-boring-meetings-ceos-want-to-ditch-sterile-zoom-c\" target=\"_blank\">some business leaders are still reluctant</a> to accept their employees' desire to leave the office behind, local governments are embracing the trend, with several U.S. <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2021-01-26/how-small-cities-are-trying-to-lure-remote-workers\" target=\"_blank\">cities</a> and <a href=\"https://wajr.com/morgantown-one-of-three-cities-selected-for-remote-work-initiative/\" target=\"_blank\">states</a> – along with <a href=\"https://www.etiasvisa.com/etias-news/digital-nomad-visas-eu-countries\" target=\"_blank\">countries</a> around the world – developing plans to attract remote workers. </p>\n<p>This migration, whether domestic or international, has the potential to enrich communities and cultivate more satisfying work lives.<img alt=\"The Conversation\" height=\"1\" src=\"https://counter.theconversation.com/content/157485/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-basic\" width=\"1\" /></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/rachael-a-woldoff-1195801\">Rachael A. Woldoff</a>, Professor of Sociology, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/west-virginia-university-1375\">West Virginia University</a></em> and <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/robert-litchfield-1219298\">Robert Litchfield</a>, Associate Professor of Business, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/washington-and-jefferson-college-5134\">Washington &amp; Jefferson College</a></em></p>\n<p>This article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license. Read the <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/what-inspired-digital-nomads-to-flee-americas-big-cities-may-spur-legions-of-remote-workers-to-do-the-same-157485\">original article</a>.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/H31uPmyNvTw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sun, 18 Apr 2021 19:31:58 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzk4OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMjY3MTQ1Nn0.aR_ROcBjLvtc-EcW-aBEFehwmVkSomehqbVFDpiAQn0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C60%2C0%2C299&height=700","linkMd5":"d9529e809c550cc32cc4e213cd9adac5","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn20@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-375_afae52800daa0cfe.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":52924,"destBytes":52924,"author":"Rachael A. Woldoff & Robert Litchfield","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2Nzk4OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMjY3MTQ1Nn0.aR_ROcBjLvtc-EcW-aBEFehwmVkSomehqbVFDpiAQn0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C60%2C0%2C299&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn20@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-375_afae52800daa0cfe.webp","https://counter.theconversation.com/content/157485/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-basic":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn76@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-19-218_e344acf5d7592015.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/H31uPmyNvTw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn49@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-18-792_0750e34d881b021d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333837},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"160-million-year-old ‘Monkeydactyl’ was the first animal to develop opposable thumbs","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/pterosaur","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzcxNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDk2MzQ2N30.jdFOi9RHcGX9KPiErt8HqL_H84rvEX-ScNYsxlC90XU/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C58%2C0%2C329&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>The 'Monkeydactly', or <em>Kunpengopterus antipollicatus,</em> was a species of pterosaur, a group of flying reptiles that were the first vertebrates to evolve the ability of powered flight.</li>\n <li>In a recent study, a team of researchers used microcomputed tomography scanning to analyze the anatomy of the newly discovered species, finding that it was the first known species to develop opposable thumbs.</li>\n <li>As highly specialized dinosaurs, pterosaurs boasted unusual anatomy that gave them special advantages as aerial predators in the Mesozoic Era.<em><em><em></em><strong></strong></em></em></li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p>A newly discovered flying dinosaur nicknamed \"Monkeydactyl\" is the oldest known creature that evolved opposable thumbs, according to new research published in <a href=\"https://www.cell.com/current-biology/fulltext/S0960-9822(21)00369-9?_returnURL=https%3A%2F%2Flinkinghub.elsevier.com%2Fretrieve%2Fpii%2FS0960982221003699%3Fshowall%3Dtrue\"
