target=\"_blank\"><em>Current Biology</em></a><em>.</em><br /></p>\n<p>The 160-million-year-old reptile is officially named <em>Kunpengopterus antipollicatus</em>. Discovered in China, the dinosaur was a darwinopteran pterosaur, a subgroup of pterosaurs, which first appeared 215 million years ago during the Triassic Period. Pterosaurs, like the pterodactyl, were the first vertebrates to evolve the ability of powered flight. </p>\n<p>But unlike other pterosaurs, the Monkeydactyl was the only species in its group known to have opposable thumbs. It's a rare adaptation for non-mammals: The only extant examples are chameleons and some species of tree frogs. (Most birds have at least one opposable digit, though that digit is usually classified as a hallux, not a pollex, which means \"thumb\" in Latin.)</p>\n<p>To analyze the anatomy of <em>K. antipollicatus, </em>an international team of researchers used microcomputed tomography scanning, which generates images of the inside of the body.</p>\n<p>\"The fingers of 'Monkeydactyl' are tiny and partly embedded in the slab,\" study co-author Fion Waisum Ma said in a <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2021-04/uob-njf041221.php\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">press release</a>. \"Thanks to micro-CT scanning, we could see through the rocks, create digital models, and tell how the opposed thumb articulates with the other finger bones.\"</p>\n<p>\"This is an interesting discovery. It provides the earliest evidence of a true opposed thumb, and it is from a pterosaur — which wasn't known for having an opposed thumb.\"</p>\n<p>As a tree-dwelling reptile, the Monkeydactyl probably evolved opposable thumbs so it could grasp tree branches, which would have helped it hang, avoid falls, and obtain food. This <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion#:~:text=Arboreal%20locomotion%20is%20the%20locomotion,but%20others%20are%20exclusively%20arboreal.\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">arboreal (tree-dwelling) locomotion</a> would help the Monkeydactyl adapt to its home ecosystem, the subtropical forests of the Tiaojishan Formation in China during the Jurassic Period.</p>\n<p>The researchers noted that the forests of the Tiaojishan Formation were likely warm and humid, thriving with \"a rich and complex\" diversity of tree-dwelling animals. But while the forests were home to multiple pterosaur species, the Monkeydactyl was likely the only one that was arboreal, spending most of its time in the treetops, while other pterosaurs occupied different levels of the forest.</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"3f40d4ee70617aa8ffb95327c7be4765\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzcxOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0OTEzMzIwNX0.kkhNMX3Xl1Dfy6N6a8t85eQEFSSf6CZlFG6YPs_aSgs/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"754\" id=\"9b7ea\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20750%20754'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"750\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">K. antipollicatus and its phylogenetic position. (A and B) Holotype specimen BPMC 0042 (A) and a schematic skeletal drawing (B). Scale bars, 50 mm.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.cell.com/current-biology/fulltext/S0960-9822(21)00369-9?_returnURL=https%3A%2F%2Flinkinghub.elsevier.com%2Fretrieve%2Fpii%2FS0960982221003699%3Fshowall%3Dtrue\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Zhou et al.</a></small></p>\n<p>This process — in which competing species manage to coexist by using the environment in different ways — is called \"niche partitioning.\"<br /></p>\n<p>\"Tiaojishan palaeoforest is home to many organisms, including three genera of darwinopteran pterosaurs,\" study author Xuanyu Zhou said in the press release. \"Our results show that <em>K. antipollicatus</em> has occupied a different niche from Darwinopterus and Wukongopterus, which has likely minimized competition among these pterosaurs.\"</p>\n<p>In general, pterosaurs are a prime example of how animals can evolve remarkably specialized adaptations. As pioneers of vertebrate flight, pterosaurs had strong and lightweight skeletons that ranged widely in size, with some boasting wingspans of more than 30 feet. The largest pterosaurs weighed more than 650 pounds and had jaws twice the length of <em>Tyrannosaurus rex. </em></p>\n<p>Unlike birds, which jump into the air using only their hind limbs, pterosaurs used their exceptionally strong hind limbs and forelimbs to push off the ground and gain enough launch power for flight. That these massive dinosaurs managed to fly, and did so successfully for about 80 million years, has long fascinated and puzzled scientists.</p>The recent discovery shows that pterosaurs developed even more remarkable adaptations than previously thought, suggesting there's still more to learn about the \"\n<a href=\"https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/pterosaurs-were-monsters-of-the-mesozoic-skies/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">monsters of the Mesozoic skies</a>.\"\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/s82-SKRDHZI\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 15 Apr 2021 22:22:05 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzcxNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDk2MzQ2N30.jdFOi9RHcGX9KPiErt8HqL_H84rvEX-ScNYsxlC90XU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C58%2C0%2C329&height=700","linkMd5":"7d29bd183d9401c8c14572181697dbdd","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn40@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-17-504_ddfa6a9d74965185.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":62912,"destBytes":62912,"author":"Stephen Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzcxNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDk2MzQ2N30.jdFOi9RHcGX9KPiErt8HqL_H84rvEX-ScNYsxlC90XU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C58%2C0%2C329&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn40@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-17-504_ddfa6a9d74965185.webp","https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20750%20754'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/s82-SKRDHZI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn37@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-18-392_c629f84457aba2ee.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333837},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"CRISPR: Can we control it?","link":"https://bigthink.com/videos/crispr-can-we-control-it","description":"<iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"100%\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.rebelmouse.com/res/scraper/embed/?jwplayer_video_url=https%3A%2F%2Fcontent.jwplatform.com%2Fplayers%2F8aqHapB5-FvQKszTI.js\" width=\"100%\"></iframe>\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>CRISPR (Clustered Regularly Interspaced Short Palindromic Repeats) is a revolutionary technology that gives scientists the ability to alter DNA. On the one hand, this tool could mean the elimination of certain diseases. On the other, there are concerns (both ethical and practical) about its misuse and the yet-unknown consequences of such experimentation.</li>\n <li>\"The technique could be misused in horrible ways,\" says counter-terrorism expert Richard A. Clarke. Clarke lists biological weapons as one of the potential threats, \"Threats for which we don't have any known antidote.\" CRISPR co-inventor, biochemist Jennifer Doudna, echos the concern, recounting a nightmare involving the technology, eugenics, and a meeting with Adolf Hitler.</li>\n <li>Should humanity even have access to this type of tool? Do the positives outweigh the potential dangers? How could something like this ever be regulated, and should it be? These questions and more are considered by Doudna, Clarke, evolutionary biologist Richard Dawkins, psychologist Steven Pinker, and physician Siddhartha Mukherjee.</li>\n</ul>\n<div>\n <br />\n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/in7r59wnRhM\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sun, 18 Apr 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"298a993c605be2c896c59851ece3f811","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Big Think","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/in7r59wnRhM":null},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333838},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Is your child's smart toy actually a creepy surveillance tool?","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/smart-toys-surveillance","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NDc5NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM1ODU2M30.XKm6YX_zbdFJGkg5nLYaD0oGYqXDVYLLYy2jHqKZrKo/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C74%2C0%2C587&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>The market for smart toys is rapidly expanding and could grow to <a href=\"https://www.juniperresearch.com/press/press-releases/smart-toy-revenues-grow-almost-200pc-by-2023\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">$18 billion</a> by 2023.</li>\n <li>Smart toys can help with learning but pose risks if they are not designed to protect children's data and safety.</li>\n <li>Many companies are developing smart toys ethically and responsibly, with makers of AI-powered smart toys encouraged to apply to the <a href=\"https://www.smarttoyawards.org/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Smart Toy Awards</a>.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p>Imagine a child born this year who will be surrounded by technology at every phase of her childhood. When she is three years old, Sophie's parents buy her a smart doll that uses facial recognition and artificial intelligence (AI) to watch, listen to, and learn from her.</p>\n<p>Like many children, Sophie will come to love this toy. And like previous generations of children with a favorite doll or teddy bear, she will carry it around with her, talk with it, and sleep with it beside her for many years.</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>If the smart doll is designed responsibly, this toy could be her best friend; if not, it will be a surveillance tool that records her every move and word spoken in its presence by her, her friends, and even her parents.</p>\n<p>Smart toys use AI to learn about the child user and personalize the play or learning experience for them. They can learn a child's favourite colour, song, and learn to recognize that child and other familiar people in that child's life. While this may sound futuristic, there are many smart toys that already provide these capabilities. The market for these toys is rapidly expanding and will grow to <a href=\"https://www.juniperresearch.com/press/press-releases/smart-toy-revenues-grow-almost-200pc-by-2023\" target=\"_blank\">$18 billion</a> by 2023.</p>\n<p>To address this urgent use of AI, the World Economic Forum recently launched the <a href=\"https://www.smarttoyawards.org/\" target=\"_blank\">Smart Toy Awards</a> to recognize ethically and responsibly designed AI-powered toys that create an innovative and healthy play experience for children.</p>\n<p>Smart toys provide enormous promise for children. They can customize learning based on data they gather about children; they can teach computer programming skills to children; and they can help children with disabilities develop cognitive, motor, and social skills.</p>\n<p>But at the same time, smart toys provide large potential risks if they are not designed to protect children's data, safety, and cybersecurity.</p>\n<h2>A cautionary tale</h2>\n<p>The example of Sophie's smart doll is not far-fetched. In 2017, <a href=\"https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2018/05/generation-ai-what-happens-when-your-childs-invisible-friend-is-an-ai-toy-that-talks-back/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">My Friend Cayla</a> – an early smart toy that used facial and voice recognition – was declared an illegal surveillance tool in many countries.</p>\n<p>If the Cayla doll was connected to a phone, data was sent to the manufacturer and a third-party company for processing and storage. And anyone with the My Friend Cayla app on their phone within 30 feet of a toy could access the toy and listen to the child user.</p>\n<p>Germany issued a \"<a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/germany-issues-kill-order-for-a-domestic-spycayla-the-toy-doll-1492098755\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">kill order</a>\" for the doll and required parents to destroy it \"with a hammer.\" Today, the only surviving Cayla dolls in Germany reside in the Spy Museum in Berlin.</p>\n<h2>The risks posed by smart toys</h2>\n<p>Sophie applies to college when she is 18 years old. If her smart doll had collected data on her from the age of 3 to 9, the company who built the toy could know her better than her parents. Without adequate data protections, the company could also sell this data to the colleges to which she is applying or other third parties.</p>\n<p>After college, Sophie applies to a job. If the employer bought data gathered on Sophie as a child, they could learn about her strengths and weaknesses. What if Sophie bullied her younger sister, yelled at her parents, or refused to do her homework as a child? All these actions conducted in the privacy of the family's home could be known by the company and sold to third parties who could use this information to discriminate against Sophie. The family's life is no longer private.</p>\n<p>Today, data is gold but gathering data on children is inherently problematic. As a company gathers data about children through Sophie's doll, they may have a responsibility to act or intervene. Imagine that Sophie tells her doll about suicidal thoughts and self-harm. Should the company be required to alert the parents and call 911?</p>\n<p>The more data that a smart toy gathers the more complex scenarios smart toy companies will likely face. Every company designing a smart toy with the capabilities to gather this information must consider these worst-case scenarios as they develop toys to protect the safety of the child user and those around them.</p>\n<h2>Developing responsible and ethical smart toys</h2>\n<p>Despite these significant risks, ethical and responsible smart toys <em>are</em> being developed. The <a href=\"https://www.smarttoyawards.org/\" target=\"_blank\">Smart Toy Awards</a> have developed <a href=\"https://www.smarttoyawards.org/learn-more.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">four key governance criteria</a> for companies developing AI-powered toys: data privacy and cybersecurity; accessibility and transparency; age appropriateness; and healthy play.</p>\n<p>Sophie's smart doll illustrates the importance of strong data privacy and clearly communicating to adults buying the toy what the smart doll does and how it operates. This must be communicated in the Terms of Service in language understandable by non-technologically literate audiences. At minimum, Smart Toys should meet <a href=\"https://www.ftc.gov/enforcement/rules/rulemaking-regulatory-reform-proceedings/childrens-online-privacy-protection-rule\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">COPPA</a> requirements in the US and <a href=\"https://gdpr-info.eu/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">GDPR</a> in the EU.</p>\n<p>Parents and guardians should understand with whom children's data is being shared and for what purpose. Companies should empower parents, guardians, and children to make their own decisions about how children's data is being used. And companies should not sell children's data to third parties.</p>\n<p>Data privacy is a foundation for ethical and responsible smart toys, but they must also be designed to be accessible, transparent, age appropriate, and promote healthy play and children's mental health.</p>\n<h2>The future of childhood</h2>\n<p>Sophie's doll doesn't necessarily pose concern for her and her parents, and data collected on her won't hinder her future if the data is carefully protected. In the EU, GDPR provides the <a href=\"https://gdpr-info.eu/art-17-gdpr/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">right to be forgotten</a>, and a similar policy could allow children like Sophie to request that all data collected on them as children by their smart toys be deleted when they turn 18 years old, so they would have a fresh start as they begin adulthood.</p>\n<p>Sophie and all children should have a fair shot at childhood, education, careers, and life. The data collected on them as children should not be used to discriminate against them in the future.</p>\n<p>Smart toys like Sophie's doll can play a pivotal role in childhoods, catalyzing creativity and critical thinking skills. Many companies are developing smart toys with careful consideration for ethics and responsibility. We urge companies to adopt our <a href=\"https://www.smarttoyawards.org/learn-more.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">governance criteria</a> as they're designing and developing smart toys.</p>\n<p>Childhood is a sacred time and parents will do everything they can to protect their children's experiences. This won't be possible unless stakeholders work together across the private, public, and nonprofit sectors to develop ethical, responsible, and innovative smart toys that protect and foster the essence of childhood.</p>\n<p>Reprinted with permission of the <a href=\"https://www.weforum.org/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">World Economic Forum</a>. Firth-Butterfield","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NDc5NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM1ODU2M30.XKm6YX_zbdFJGkg5nLYaD0oGYqXDVYLLYy2jHqKZrKo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C74%2C0%2C587&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn74@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-512_9c0947a7aab71f7e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/5MnZWNLqG-U":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn68@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-18-418_40e37d63b2151492.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333843},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Mysterious vomiting disease in dogs is due to novel coronavirus","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/coronavirus-in-dogs","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTY5MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDk1NzY2NX0.Uc8EOkhD_2Xksb7hIw0qk27TU9ROLCBihSrnFu-S-i8/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C229%2C0%2C229&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>A different coronavirus outbreak in late 2019 made many dogs in the UK very ill.</li>\n <li><span></span>The strangeness of the disease led veterinarians to send questionnaires to their peers and pet owners.</li>\n <li>The findings point toward the need for better systems to identify disease outbreaks in animals.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>A recent study suggests that a mysterious disease plaguing dogs in the UK is caused by a novel coronavirus. This virus, which coincidentally appeared in late 2019 and began to concern veterinarians in early 2020, is not related to the virus which causes COVID-19, but can make your four-legged friend feel quite ill.</p>\n<h2>Novel coronavirus in dogs</h2>\n<p>The term \"<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronavirus\" target=\"_blank\"><u>coronavirus</u></a>\" doesn't refer to a single disease, but a family of viruses (more formally, <em>Coronaviridae</em>) that share a shape similar to a crown (hence the name, \"corona\"). They infect many different kinds of animals and cause various diseases from COVID-19 and SARS to the common cold.</p>\n<p>This new coronavirus, a variant of canine enteric coronavirus, was first noticed in January 2020 when a veterinarian in the United Kingdom treated \"an unusually high number\" of dogs with severe vomiting and other gastrointestinal issues at his office. Concerned about this spike in doggy indigestion, they reached out to other veterinarians to see if they were reporting a similar outbreak.</p>\n<p>Online questionnaires were sent out to more than a thousand vets and pet owners to map the outbreak and collect information on which animals were being affected. Analysis of this data showed that nearly all of the cases involved vomiting and a loss of appetite, and half of them also involved diarrhea. Most of the cases took place in south and northwest England, though a large outbreak also occurred in and around the Scottish city of Edinburgh.</p>\n<p>The data also suggested that male dogs in contact with other dogs were most likely to be infected, hinting at \"either transmission between dogs or a common environmental source.\" The dogs recovered in more than 99 percent of cases.</p>\n<p>Hoping to move beyond the questionnaire, the authors also turned to records to piece together what happened.</p>\n<p>While public health data for animals is less frequently gathered than it is for humans, electronic records of pet admissions to veterinarian offices and pet insurance payouts do exist. The researchers accessed these records and found that the number of dogs recorded with stomach problems rose between December 2019 and March 2020, with nearly double the number of expected cases occurring during that time. There was also a concomitant rise in prescriptions for drugs to treat those conditions.</p>\n<p>A later comparison of samples from dogs that were sick and healthy control dogs confirmed the presence of the novel coronavirus in the ill dogs. All of this was later compiled into a<a href=\"https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/2/20-2452_article\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>study</u></a> that was recently published in <em>Emerging Infectious Diseases</em>, which is produced by the CDC.</p>\n<h2>Should I be concerned?</h2>\n<div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\">\n <iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/4qEqesmUWio\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe>\n</div>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>This coronavirus only affects dogs, and the researchers didn't find anything to suggest that humans could become infected. </p>\n<p>However, the scale of the outbreak and the lack of tools immediately available to determine what was happening led the researchers to suggest that better organization is needed. Many of the authors are involved in creating a disease surveillance system for dogs, known as <a href=\"https://www.liverpool.ac.uk/savsnet/savsnet-agile/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>SAVSNet-Agile</u></a>.</p>\n<p>The authors also mention that \"previous CeCoV [canine enteric coronavirus] seasonality suggests further outbreaks may occur.\" Thanks to this study, your local vet might be a little more prepared for it next time.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/DhSjrXq3WLY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 20 Apr 2021 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTY5MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDk1NzY2NX0.Uc8EOkhD_2Xksb7hIw0qk27TU9ROLCBihSrnFu-S-i8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C229%2C0%2C229&height=700","linkMd5":"d4dce435a56cd53b8d52cd5278fa420a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn39@2020_1/2021/04/23/17-38-17-476_6572197237250a31.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":163900,"destBytes":163900,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTY5MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDk1NzY2NX0.Uc8EOkhD_2Xksb7hIw0qk27TU9ROLCBihSrnFu-S-i8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C229%2C0%2C229&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn39@2020_1/2021/04/23/17-38-17-476_6572197237250a31.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/DhSjrXq3WLY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn75@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-18-411_6656c4e43491328b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333839},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Study: People will donate more to charity if they think something’s in it for them","link":"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/altruism-charity","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzQ1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NTAzMjg4N30.Ge1RnKZI899wVxCGsvrxo9zWIXM7WZ2nrrU08lQFgKs/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C260%2C0%2C260&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>A study finds asking for donations by appealing to the donor's self-interest may result in more money than appealing to their better nature. </li>\n <li>Those who received an appeal to self-interest were both more likely to give and gave more than those in the control group. </li>\n <li>The effect was most pronounced for those who hadn't given before.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p>Even the best charities with the longest records of doing great fundraising work have to spend some time making sure that the next donation checks will keep coming in. One way to do this is by showing potential donors all the good things the charity did over the previous year. But there may be a better way.</p>\n<p>A new study by researchers in the United States and Australia suggests that appealing to the benefits people will receive themselves after a donation nudges them to donate more money than appealing to the greater good.</p>\n<h2>How to get people to give away free money</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"fdff99aab7493f259b7625950d28ca49\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzQ0OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MDAxODUyNX0.9hP26SsJ75bRKnK84qo9E6AmauHmONZj0VeGTGVSyQ4/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"451\" id=\"06f73\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20801%20451'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"801\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">The postcards that were sent to different study subjects. The one on the left highlighted benefits to the self, while the one on the right highlighted benefits to others.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">List et al. / Nature Human Behaviour </small></p>\n<p>The <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41562-021-01095-8\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>study</u></a>, published in <em>Nature Human Behaviour</em>, utilized the <a href=\"https://www.pickclickgive.org/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>Pick.Click.Give</u></a> program in Alaska. This program allows Alaska residents who qualify for dividends from the Alaska Permanent Fund, a yearly payment ranging from $800 to $2000 in recent years, to donate a portion of it to various in-state non-profit organizations.</p>\n<p>The researchers randomly assigned households to either a control group or to receive a postcard in the mail encouraging them to donate a portion of their dividend to charity. That postcard could come in one of two forms, either highlighting the benefits to others or the benefits to themselves.</p>\n<p>Those who got the postcard touting self-benefits were 6.6 percent more likely to give than those in the control group and gave 23 percent more on average. Those getting the benefits-to-others postcard were slightly more likely to give than those receiving no postcard, but their donations were no larger.</p>\n<p>Additionally, the researchers were able to break the subject list down into a \"warm list\" of those who had given at least once before in the last two years and a \"cold list\" of those who had not. Those on the warm list, who were already giving, saw only minor increases in their likelihood to donate after getting a postcard in the mail compared to those on the cold list. </p>\n<p>Additionally, the researchers found that warm-list subjects who received the self-interest postcard gave 11 percent more than warm-list subjects in the control group. Amazingly, among cold-list subjects, those who received a self-interest postcard gave 39 percent more.</p>\n<p>These are substantial improvements. At the end of the study, the authors point out, \"If we had sent the benefits to self message to all households in the state, aggregate contributions would have increased by nearly US$600,000.\"</p>\n<p>To put this into perspective, in 2017 the <a href=\"https://www.pickclickgive.org/about-pick-click-give/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>total donations</u></a> to the program were roughly $2,700,000.</p>\n<h2>Is altruism dead?</h2>\n<div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\">\n <iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/xn8o3h-B4vU\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe>\n</div>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>Are all actions inherently self-interested? Thankfully, no. The study focuses entirely on effective ways to increase charitable donations above levels that currently exist. It doesn't deny that some people are giving out of pure altruism, but rather that an appeal based on self-interest is effective. Plenty of people were giving before this study took place who didn't need a postcard as encouragement. It is also possible that some people donated part of their dividend check to a charity that does not work with Pick.Click.Give and were uncounted here. </p>\n<p>It is also important to note that Pick.Click.Give does not provide services but instead gives money to a wide variety of organizations that do. Those organizations operate in fields from animal rescue to job training to public broadcasting. The authors note that it is possible that a more specific appeal to the benefits others will receive from a donation might prove more effective than the generic and all-inclusive \"Make Alaska Better For Everyone\" appeal that they used.</p>\n<p>In an ideal world, charity is its own reward. In ours, it might help to remind somebody how <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warm-glow_giving\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>warm and fuzzy</u></a> they'll feel after donating to your cause.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/DD_y83CRoOA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 16 Apr 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzQ1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NTAzMjg4N30.Ge1RnKZI899wVxCGsvrxo9zWIXM7WZ2nrrU08lQFgKs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C260%2C0%2C260&height=700","linkMd5":"c8afe29a20bc5d0d449baea8b88cdeaf","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":95324,"destBytes":95324,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1NzQ1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NTAzMjg4N30.Ge1RnKZI899wVxCGsvrxo9zWIXM7WZ2nrrU08lQFgKs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C260%2C0%2C260&height=700":null,"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20801%20451'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/DD_y83CRoOA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn2@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-18-392_c3ea5dfe619dbdf9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333841},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Neuroplasticity can be turned on and off in the brain of a fruit fly","link":"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/neuroplasticity","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTE3Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDgyNTYxNH0.A5w1AWJtbqyPM2EyMbNZR0SLQKIwxNyNmt9ek8XFv_M/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C62%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<h2></h2>\n<p>Neuroplasticity – the ability of neurons to change their structure and function in response to experiences – can be turned off and on by the cells that surround neurons in the brain, according to a new study on fruit flies that I co-authored.</p>\n<hr />\n<h2>The big idea</h2>\n<p>As fruit fly larvae age, their neurons shift from a highly adaptable state to a stable state and lose their ability to change. During this process, support cells in the brain – called astrocytes – <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuron.2017.09.056\" target=\"_blank\">envelop the parts of the neurons</a> that send and receive electrical information. When my team removed the astrocytes, the neurons in the fruit fly larvae remained plastic longer, hinting that somehow astrocytes suppress a neuron's ability to change. We then discovered two specific proteins that regulate neuroplasticity.</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"354d5778bb97d01dd3345c63ae5b93bc\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTEyMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MzA3MTIxM30.MfroMUkYXXusvqqIE_oWaQJbtgQboXT7GDTT5tsvLGg/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"488\" id=\"a9a41\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' viewBox='0 0 754 488'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"754\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Sarah DeGenova Ackerman, <a href=\"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nd/4.0/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-ND</a></small></p>\n<h2>Why it matters</h2>\n<p>The human brain is made up of billions of neurons that form complex connections with one another. Flexibility at these connections is a <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1820836117\" target=\"_blank\">major driver of learning and memory</a>, but things can go wrong if it isn't tightly regulated. For example, in people, too much plasticity at the wrong time is linked to brain disorders such as <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cub.2015.09.040\" target=\"_blank\">epilepsy</a> and <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/S0896-6273(00)81109-5\" target=\"_blank\">Alzheimer's disease</a>. Additionally, reduced levels of the two neuroplasticity-controlling proteins we identified are linked to increased susceptibility to <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.3389/fncel.2018.00470\" target=\"_blank\">autism</a> and <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/s41380-020-00944-8\" target=\"_blank\">schizophrenia</a>.</p>\n<p>Similarly, in our fruit flies, removing the cellular brakes on plasticity permanently impaired their crawling behavior. While fruit flies are of course different from humans, their brains work in very similar ways to the human brain and can offer valuable insight.</p>\n<p>One obvious benefit of discovering the effect of these proteins is the potential to treat some neurological diseases. But since a neuron's flexibility is closely tied to learning and memory, in theory, researchers might be able to <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1111/nyas.12682\" target=\"_blank\">boost plasticity</a> in a controlled way to <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1098/rstb.2013.0288\" target=\"_blank\">enhance cognition in adults</a>. This could, for example, allow people to more easily learn a new language or musical instrument. </p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"bfcd471256f7a963a68374bfd69c5f7d\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTEyNS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDI1NTEyMn0.xCaHkgqe-B3OF_dMnxGSBwsNxJ4aroUQn4fm3Pr-xzI/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"430\" id=\"5d5f9\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' viewBox='0 0 754 430'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"754\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">In this image showing a developing fruit fly brain on the right and the attached nerve cord on the left, the astrocytes are labeled in different colors showing their wide distribution among neurons.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Sarah DeGenova Ackerman, <a href=\"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nd/4.0/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-ND</a></small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<h2>How we did the work</h2>\n<p><a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/citations?user=-sssMIEAAAAJ&amp;hl=en&amp;oi=sra\" target=\"_blank\">My colleagues and I</a> focused our experiments on a specific type of neurons called motor neurons. These control movements like <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1186/s13064-018-0103-z\" target=\"_blank\">crawling</a> and <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1002/cne.903400311\" target=\"_blank\">flying</a> in fruit flies. To figure out how astrocytes controlled neuroplasticity, we used genetic tools to turn off specific proteins in the astrocytes one by one and then measured the effect on motor neuron structure. We found that astrocytes and motor neurons communicate with one another using a specific pair of proteins called neuroligins and neurexins. These proteins essentially function as an off button for <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-03441-2\" target=\"_blank\">motor neuron plasticity</a>.</p>\n<h2>What still isn't known</h2>\n<p>My team discovered that two proteins can control neuroplasticity, but we don't know how these cues from astrocytes cause neurons to lose their ability to change.</p>\n<p>Additionally, researchers still know very little about why neuroplasticity is so strong in younger animals and <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1820836117\" target=\"_blank\">relatively weak in adulthood</a>. In our study, we showed that prolonging plasticity beyond development can sometimes be <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-03441-2\" target=\"_blank\">harmful to behavior</a>, but we don't yet know why that is, either. </p>\n<h2>What's next</h2>\n<p>I want to explore why longer periods of neuroplasticity can be harmful. Fruit flies are great study organisms for this research because it is very easy to <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/nmeth.1567\" target=\"_blank\">modify the neural connections in their brains</a>. In my team's next project, we hope to determine how changes in neuroplasticity during development can lead to long–term changes in behavior.</p>\n<p>There is so much more work to be done, but our research is a first step toward treatments that use astrocytes to influence how neurons change in the mature brain. If researchers can understand the basic mechanisms that control neuroplasticity, they will be one step closer to developing therapies to treat a variety of neurological disorders.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/sarah-degenova-ackerman-1223343\" target=\"_blank\">Sarah DeGenova Ackerman</a>, Postdoctoral Fellow, UO Institute of Neuroscience and Howard Hughes Medical Institute, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/university-of-oregon-811\" target=\"_blank\">University of Oregon</a></em></p>\n<h2></h2>\n<p>This article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\" target=\"_blank\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license. Read the <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/astrocyte-cells-in-the-fruit-fly-brain-are-an-on-off-switch-that-controls-when-neurons-can-change-and-grow-158601\" target=\"_blank\">original article</a>.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/fP2-n02D1h4\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 19 Apr 2021 DeGenova Ackerman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3MTE3Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDgyNTYxNH0.A5w1AWJtbqyPM2EyMbNZR0SLQKIwxNyNmt9ek8XFv_M/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C62%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn88@2020_1/2021/04/23/17-38-17-473_7ac1094477c48221.webp","https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' viewBox='0 0 754 488'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' viewBox='0 0 754 430'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/fP2-n02D1h4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn91@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-18-748_0807dcd5f13b7ac6.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333841},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"False positive: Exoplanets could have lots of oxygen but no life","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/exoplanets-oxygen","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NzgxMy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzA2MTY2M30.FmJ4exTmxjbpmygCZeEVsUkRN7CrehF74kdqarTHUTE/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>If an exoplanet houses life, it almost certainly will have gaseous oxygen.</li>\n <li>But a new study modeling the development of rocky planets identifies three scenarios in which oxygen can form abiotically. </li>\n <li>The notion that oxygenated exoplanets are all candidates to host life should be treated with skepticism. </li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p>Research that aims to identify exoplanets that might contain life usually use oxygen as a biomarker. But a new<a href=\"https://agupubs.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1029/2020AV000294\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>study</u></a> published in <em>AGU Advances</em> explains that this can be very misleading: Oxygen can easily accumulate in an exoplanet's atmosphere without any biological origin.</p>\n<p>Oxygen is considered a biomarker because photosynthesis — the process by which plants use sunlight to fix carbon dioxide into sugar — produces oxygen as a waste product. Thus, a planet with oxygen in its atmosphere is considered a strong candidate to host some kind of lifeform.</p>\n<p>The team, led by Joshua Krissansen‐Totton of UC Santa Cruz, developed a model of planetary formation that allowed them to tinker with variables that could affect how an Earth-like planet develops. Using their model, the researchers were consistently able to produce three scenarios in which an Earth-like planet has levels of oxygen in its atmosphere similar to ours, but life was not part of the formula.</p>\n<h2>Three oxygenated worlds with no life</h2>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"819770a879b78f8e6329fade5fa2f198\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NzgxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MTg1NjAwMH0.gzhVa09PLjVl6wc33lq_LirtBzNVe8kgrJLyXWr-0UU/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"1180\" id=\"eb74e\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%202098%201180'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"2098\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">The planetary evolution model. Arrows show the flow of certain substances and heat energy between different layers of the Earth and its atmosphere.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Krissansen-Totton et al./ AGU Advances</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>In the first scenario, an exoplanet has very high levels of carbon dioxide and water in the atmosphere. Under these conditions, a strong greenhouse gas effect means there will be no water on the exoplanet's surface. When hit by ultraviolet (UV) light, water vapor in the upper atmosphere can occasionally split into hydrogen and oxygen. The lighter hydrogen gas escapes into space, leaving the heavier oxygen gas behind. </p>\n<p>In the second scenario, an exoplanet \"waterworld\" contains anywhere from 10 to 230 times as much water as the Earth has today. Under these conditions, the<a href=\"https://www.universetoday.com/61080/oxygen-cycle/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>oxygen cycle</u></a> — which involves the circulation of oxygen through the atmosphere, lifeforms, and rocks — essentially doesn't exist. Pressure from the massive oceans on the crust would shut down the geological activity necessary to recycle oxygen, leaving it in the atmosphere. </p>\n<p>In the third scenario, an exoplanet \"desertworld\" has conditions exactly the opposite of those in the \"waterworld.\" This type of exoplanet has very little water, no more than a third of what Earth has in its oceans. Under these conditions, the molten surface of a young exoplanet can freeze while the limited water supply is still found only as steam (vapor) in the atmosphere. This prevents oxygen from being absorbed by the crust. Then, as with the first scenario, UV light breaks up water into hydrogen and oxygen.</p>\n<h2>Implications for the hunt for E.T.</h2>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"b80ad392243f4f953b89bd0db7737892\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NzgwOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MzU3NDI5Mn0.-dAf49ZSF1yAW9U_b2s0yXcv8SeOBkMbplg0X2XqxHk/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"1080\" id=\"5ef94\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%201080'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1440\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">An infographic illustratingthe three planets described above and how they might form.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Illustration by J. Krissansen-Totton</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>None of the three scenarios assures an oxygen-rich atmosphere; they simply allow for oxygen to occur abiotically. Professor Krissansen‐Totton described the utility of the model in a <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2021-04/uoc--swo041321.php\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>press release</u></a>:</p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 60px;\">\"This is useful because it shows there are ways to get oxygen in the atmosphere without life, but there are other observations you can make to help distinguish these false positives from the real deal. For each scenario, we try to say what your telescope would need to be able to do to distinguish this from biological oxygen.\"</p>\n<p>Such telescopes should be in orbit by 2030. Now the scientists using them know what to look for.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/FnoQL8RZIn4\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 19 Apr 2021 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NzgxMy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzA2MTY2M30.FmJ4exTmxjbpmygCZeEVsUkRN7CrehF74kdqarTHUTE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"ab54f57f885f33b25f8d4cb30d202734","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn31@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-518_0e6f938b72a82196.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":67364,"destBytes":67364,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NzgxMy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzA2MTY2M30.FmJ4exTmxjbpmygCZeEVsUkRN7CrehF74kdqarTHUTE/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn31@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-518_0e6f938b72a82196.webp","https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%202098%201180'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%201080'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/FnoQL8RZIn4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn10@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-18-319_2d820b0d26c0b056.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333839},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Fighting online misinformation: We're doing it wrong","link":"https://bigthink.com/coronavirus/new-way-fight-online-misinformation","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4MjU1Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxOTMzOTUzM30.gT8JLMF10uYAuAeTbIplDenaOzReVdy2yKUJPG0zLqo/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C142%2C0%2C172&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Like the coronavirus, engaging with misinformation can inadvertently cause it to spread.</li>\n <li>Social media has a business model based on getting users to spend increasing amounts of time on their platforms, which is why they are hesitant to remove engaging content.</li>\n <li>The best way to fight online misinformation is to drown it out with the truth.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p><span></span>A year ago, the Center for Countering Digital Hate warned of the parallel pandemics — the biological contagion of COVID-19 and the social contagion of misinformation, aiding the spread of the disease. Since the outbreak of COVID-19, anti-vaccine accounts have gained <a href=\"https://www.counterhate.com/playbook\" target=\"_blank\">10 million new social media followers</a>, while we have witnessed <a href=\"https://news.sky.com/story/coronavirus-5g-mast-set-on-fire-after-baseless-conspiracy-theory-link-to-pandemic-11968217\" target=\"_blank\">arson attacks against 5G masts</a>, <a href=\"https://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-9184807/Man-dragged-security-trying-dying-patient-home-against-doctors-orders.html\" target=\"_blank\">hospital staff abused for treating COVID patients</a>, and conspiracists addressing crowds of thousands.</p>\n<p>Many have refused to follow guidance issued to control the spread of the virus, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2020/apr/08/influencers-being-key-distributors-of-coronavirus-fake-news\" target=\"_blank\">motivated by beliefs in falsehoods</a> about its origins and effects. The reluctance we see in some to get the COVID vaccine is greater amongst those <a href=\"https://www.counterhate.com/post/covid-vaccine-polling-reveals-links-to-age-race-and-political-values\" target=\"_blank\">who rely on social media rather than traditional media</a> for their information. In a pandemic, lies cost lives, and it has felt like a new conspiracy theory has sprung up online every day.</p>\n<p>How we, as social media users, behave in response to misinformation can either enable or prevent it from being seen and believed by more people.</p>\n<h2>The rules are different online</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"129bff11476fa4c625187585b2b51287\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4MjU4MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1NTY3NDI1OX0.djePvXKIvgGZ9FHTzXmv1w7dbHtMqHn-npfRQArgKV4/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"2333\" id=\"9324b\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/coronavirus/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%203500%202333'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"3500\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/dr-doreen-brown-receives-the-first-of-two-pfizer-biontech-news-photo/1230008516?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Pool via Getty Images</small></p>\n<p>If a colleague mentions in the office that Bill Gates planned the pandemic, or a friend at dinner tells the table that the COVID vaccine could make them infertile, the right thing to do is often to challenge their claims. We don't want anyone to be left believing these falsehoods.</p>\n<p>But digital is different. The rules of physics online are not the same as they are in the offline world. We need new solutions for the problems we face online.</p>\n<p>Now, imagine that in order to reply to your friend, you must first hand him a megaphone so that everyone within a five-block radius can hear what he has to say. It would do more damage than good, but this is essentially what we do when we engage with misinformation online.</p>\n<p>Think about misinformation as being like the coronavirus — when we engage with it, we help to spread it to everyone else with whom we come into contact. If a public figure with a large following responds to a post containing misinformation, they ensure the post is seen by hundreds of thousands or even millions of people with one click. Social media algorithms also push content into more users' newsfeeds if it appears to be engaging, so lots of interactions from users with relatively small followings can still have unintended negative consequences.</p>\n<blockquote>\n The trend of people celebrating and posting photos of themselves or loved ones receiving the vaccine has been far more effective than any attempt to disprove a baseless claim about Bill Gates or 5G mobile technology. \n</blockquote>\n<p>Additionally, whereas we know our friend from the office or dinner, most of the misinformation we see online will come from strangers. They often will be from one of two groups — true believers, whose minds are made up, and professional propagandists, who <a href=\"https://www.counterhate.com/anti-vaxx-industry\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">profit</a> from building large audiences online and selling them products (including false cures). Both of these groups use trolling tactics, that is, seeking to trigger people to respond in anger, thus helping them reach new audiences and thereby gaming the algorithm.</p>\n<p>On the day the COVID vaccine was approved in the UK, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/TheOndrakGuy/status/1334096126304247810\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">anti-vaccine activists were able to provoke pro-vaccine voices into posting about thalidomide</a>, exposing new audiences to a reason to distrust the medical establishment. Those who spread misinformation understand the rules of the game online; it's time those of us on the side of enlightenment values of truth and science did too.</p>\n<h2>How to fight online misinformation</h2>\n<p>Of course, it is much easier for social media companies to take on this issue than for us citizens. Research from the Center for Countering Digital Hate and Anti-Vax Watch last month found that <a href=\"https://www.counterhate.com/disinformationdozen\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">65% of anti-vaccine content on social media is linked to just twelve individuals</a> and their organizations. Were the platforms to simply remove the accounts of these superspreaders, it would do a huge amount to reduce harmful misinformation.</p>\n<p>The problem is that social media platforms are resistant to do so. These businesses have been built by constantly increasing the amount of time users spend on their platforms. Getting rid of the creators of engaging content that has millions of people hooked is antithetical to the business model. It will require intervention from governments to force tech companies to finally protect their users and society as a whole.</p>\n<p>So, what can the rest of us do, while we await state regulation?</p>\n<p>Instead of engaging, we should be outweighing the bad with the good. Every time you see a piece of harmful misinformation, share advice or information from a trusted source, like the WHO or BBC, on the same subject. The trend of people celebrating and posting photos of themselves or loved ones receiving the vaccine has been far more effective than any attempt to disprove a baseless claim about Bill Gates or 5G mobile technology. In the attention economy that governs tech platforms, drowning out is a better strategy than rebuttal.</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p><em>Imran Ahmed is CEO of the <a href=\"https://www.counterhate.com/our-people\" target=\"_blank\">Center for Countering Digital Hate</a>.</em></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/fjZGq3uGUqg\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 22 Apr 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4MjU1Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxOTMzOTUzM30.gT8JLMF10uYAuAeTbIplDenaOzReVdy2yKUJPG0zLqo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C142%2C0%2C172&height=700","linkMd5":"84c4b024462199484cab6edb77b07f76","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn80@2020_1/2021/04/23/17-38-17-479_dd5f6b53372f3aaa.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":19336,"destBytes":19336,"author":"Imran Ahmed","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4MjU1Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxOTMzOTUzM30.gT8JLMF10uYAuAeTbIplDenaOzReVdy2yKUJPG0zLqo/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C142%2C0%2C172&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn80@2020_1/2021/04/23/17-38-17-479_dd5f6b53372f3aaa.webp","https://bigthink.com/coronavirus/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%203500%202333'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/fjZGq3uGUqg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn19@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-18-385_da71279a1cef6b21.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333839},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Study: Tripping might not be required for psychedelic therapy","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/tripping-not-required","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzM5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjYyNjc5OX0.C3NJM4JMHvINXnok5LtUBXEiXjmr4mQC6wWC0Dgj0Ps/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>A phase 2 clinical trial by Imperial College London found psilocybin to be as effective at treating depression as escitalopram, a commonly prescribed antidepressant.</li>\n <li><span></span>A different study by the University of Maryland showed that blocking the hallucinogenic effects of magic mushrooms in mice did not reduce the antidepressant effect.</li>\n <li>Combined, these studies could lead to new ways of applying psychedelics to patient populations that don't want to trip.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p>Due to stigma, their illegal status and difficulty in finding control groups, research with psychedelics has been a challenge. But research increasingly shows that this class of drug has legitimate medicinal uses, and they may be just as good or even better than more traditional therapies.</p>\n<p>Now, the Centre for Psychedelic Research at Imperial College London reports in the <a href=\"https://www.nejm.org/doi/full/10.1056/NEJMoa2032994\" target=\"_blank\"><em>New England Journal of Medicine</em></a> that when pitted against escitalopram (brand name: Lexapro), psilocybin was as effective as the popular SSRI (selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor) in treating moderate to severe depression. Perhaps most significantly, these results were obtained when comparing 6 weeks of daily doses of escitalopram to just two administrations of psilocybin. </p>\n<p>Robin Carhart-Harris, head of the center who has published over 100 papers on psychedelics, is confident this study represents another step forward in applying psychedelics to mental health treatment protocols while also reducing fears a lot of citizens have around these substances. In a press release, <a href=\"https://www.imperial.ac.uk/news/219413/magic-mushroom-compound-performs-well-antidepressant/\" target=\"_blank\">he said</a>:</p>\n<p rel=\"margin-left: 20px;\" style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"One of the most important aspects of this work is that people can clearly see the promise of properly delivered psilocybin therapy by viewing it compared with a more familiar, established treatment in the same study. Psilocybin performed very favorably in this head-to-head.\"</p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"0eed74736c4a717b78e3d243dfb01bb1\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzQxMy9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3ODM0NjMzMH0.1SoR50QBtK476xr4tLIvHjs6g6BFVVvzQfkVM02Tjug/img.png?width=980\" height=\"868\" id=\"c85fc\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201412%20868'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1412\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"> <a href=\"https://www.nejm.org/doi/full/10.1056/NEJMoa2032994\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Robin Carhart-Harris et al, NEJM, 2021. </small></p>\n<p>As depicted above, the phase 2 clinical trial included 59 volunteers. The escitalopram (control) group received six weeks of daily escitalopram in addition to two tiny (1-mg) doses of psilocybin — a dose so low that it is unlikely to produce hallucinogenic effects. The psilocybin (experimental) group received two 25-mg doses of psilocybin three weeks apart with placebo given on all the other days.<br /></p>\n<p>At the end of the study, both groups saw a decrease in depressive symptoms, though the results were not statistically significant. (That isn't necessarily bad because if the two drugs have similar effects, then they would <em>not</em> produce statistically significant results. Still, a larger study is needed to confirm that psilocybin is \"just as good as\" escitalopram.) </p>\n<p>Additionally, several other outcomes favored psilocybin over escitalopram. For instance, 57 percent in the psilocybin group saw a remission of symptoms compared to 28 percent in the escitalopram group. This result was significant.</p>\n<h3>Psychedelics without tripping</h3>\n<p>As psychedelics become decriminalized and potentially legalized for therapeutic use, however, a large population of people might desire the antidepressant effects without the hallucinations. For example, the psychedelic ibogaine may be useful for treating addiction, so the company Mindmed is developing an analog that works without producing the unwanted hallucinogenic side effects.</p>\n<p>A <a href=\"https://www.pnas.org/content/118/17/e2022489118\" target=\"_blank\">new research article</a>, published in the journal <em>PNAS</em>, investigated the antidepressant effects of psilocybin on a group of chronically stressed mice. (Under immense stress, mice develop something resembling human depression.) As with humans, depressed mice lose a sense of joy, which can be assessed by determining their preference for sugar water over tap water. Normal mice prefer sugar water, but depressed mice simply don't care. </p>\n<p>Once the mice were no longer juicing up on the sweetened water, the team dosed them with psilocybin alongside a drug called ketanserin, a 5-HT2A serotonin receptor antagonist that eliminates psychedelic effects. Within 24 hours of receiving the dose, the mice were rushing back to the sugar water, indicating that tripping is not necessary for psilocybin to work as an antidepressant.</p>\n<p>While the team is excited about these results, they realize it needs to be replicated in a different population. </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"The possibility of combining psychedelic compounds and a 5-HT2AR antagonist offers a potential means to increase their acceptance and clinical utility and should be studied in human depression.\"</p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image image-crop-16x9\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"67646a3e32ead0208a81b6b5e56f63c9\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzQwNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyNDk5MDEzNH0.VIFu1Fyp6hWfG51kbOMAi7u3dWAG5Sw5Hn0sjmLG940/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C188%2C0%2C17&amp;height=700\" height=\"700\" id=\"e6d07\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201245%20700'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1245\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Photo: Cannabis_Pic / Adobe Stock</small></p>\n<h3>The future of psychedelic therapy<br /></h3>\n<p>Psychedelics such as psilocybin and LSD have a long track record of efficacy in clinical trials and anecdotal experiences. Almost all volunteers of the famous <a href=\"http://buquad.com/2013/02/18/remembering-the-marsh-chapel-experiment/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Marsh Chapel experiment</a> claimed their experience on Good Friday in 1962 was one of the most significant events of their lives — and this was a quarter-century after the fact. A <a href=\"https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s00213-017-4771-x\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">more recent, controlled study</a> found that a single dose of psilocybin showed antidepressant effects six months later. </p>\n<p>Proponents of macrodosing and ritualistic experiences sometimes argue that the full-blown mystical trip <em>is</em> the therapy, though this is anecdotal, not clinical research. As the Maryland team noted, a number of people are contraindicated for psychedelics, whether through a family history of schizophrenia or current antidepressant treatments. </p>\n<p>Senior author Scott Thompson is excited for future research on this topic. As <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2021-04/uoms-pem041321.php\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">he said</a> of his team's findings:</p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"The psychedelic experience is incredibly powerful and can be life-changing, but that could be too much for some people or not appropriate… These findings show that activation of the receptor causing the psychedelic effect isn't absolutely required for the antidepressant benefits, at least in mice.\"</p>\n<p>Hopefully, with more research occurring in psychedelics than even in the 1950s (when studies predominantly relied on anecdotal evidence and little government support), the longstanding stigmatization of psychedelics is beginning to recede. This could open up new possibilities for both clinical research and, for those curious about the ritual effects, a continuation of introspective experiences.</p>\n<p>--</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p><em>Stay in touch with Derek on <a href=\"http://www.twitter.com/derekberes\" target=\"_blank\">Twitter</a> and <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/DerekBeresdotcom\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Facebook</a>. His most recent book is</em> \"<em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B08KRVMP2M?pf_rd_r=MDJW43337675SZ0X00FH&amp;pf_rd_p=edaba0ee-c2fe-4124-9f5d-b31d6b1bfbee\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Hero's Dose: The Case For Psychedelics in Ritual and Therapy</a>.\"</em></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/_ogWfK77Bms\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\"
