+0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzM5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjYyNjc5OX0.C3NJM4JMHvINXnok5LtUBXEiXjmr4mQC6wWC0Dgj0Ps/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"8e9e06602fcf86752344e1fa12e60e0b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn68@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-492_734b3ecf2c673634.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":93110,"destBytes":93110,"author":"Derek Beres","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NzM5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjYyNjc5OX0.C3NJM4JMHvINXnok5LtUBXEiXjmr4mQC6wWC0Dgj0Ps/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn68@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-492_734b3ecf2c673634.webp","https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201412%20868'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201245%20700'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/_ogWfK77Bms":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn16@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-18-411_aa35b1c437fdce25.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333843},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Self-awareness is what makes us human","link":"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/self-awareness-makes-us-human","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3Nzk4My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDc5MzQ4OH0.9US6XRjm_6aDnzlqPHizuJdZUSe43zZLgUiC6eqH2dg/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C45%2C0%2C356&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Self-awareness — namely, our capacity to think about our thoughts — is central to how we perceive the world.</li>\n <li>Without self-awareness, education, literature, and other human endeavors would not be possible.</li>\n <li>Striving toward greater self-awareness is the spiritual goal of many religions and philosophies.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p><em></em><em>The following is an excerpt from Dr. Stephen Fleming's forthcoming book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Know-Thyself-Self-Awareness-Stephen-Fleming/dp/1541672844\" target=\"_blank\">Know Thyself</a>. It is reprinted with permission from the author.</em></p>\n<p>I now run a neuroscience lab dedicated to the study of self-awareness at University College London. My team is one of several working within the Wellcome Centre for Human Neuroimaging, located in an elegant town house in Queen Square in London. The basement of our building houses large machines for brain imaging, and each group in the Centre uses this technology to study how different aspects of the mind and brain work: how we see, hear, remember, speak, make decisions, and so on. The students and postdocs in my lab focus on the brain's capacity for self-awareness. I find it a remarkable fact that something unique about our biology has allowed the human brain to turn its thoughts on itself.</p>\n<p>Until quite recently, however, this all seemed like nonsense. As the nineteenth-century French philosopher Auguste Comte put it: \"The thinking individual cannot cut himself in two — one of the parts reasoning, while the other is looking on. Since in this case the organ observed and the observing organ are identical, how could any observation be made?\" In other words, how can the same brain turn its thoughts upon itself?</p>\n<p>Comte's argument chimed with scientific thinking at the time. After the Enlightenment dawned on Europe, an increasingly popular view was that self-awareness was special and not something that could be studied using the tools of science. Western philosophers were instead using self-reflection as a philosophical tool, much as mathematicians use algebra in the pursuit of new mathematical truths. René Descartes relied on self-reflection in this way to reach his famous conclusion, \"I think, therefore I am,\" noting along the way that \"I know clearly that there is nothing that can be perceived by me more easily or more clearly than my own mind.\" Descartes proposed that a central soul was the seat of thought and reason, commanding our bodies to act on our behalf. The soul could not be split in two — it just was. Self-awareness was therefore mysterious and indefinable, and off-limits to science.</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d42c844ad828aa444fbf1222a25f9160\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3Nzk5Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNzk2MzUyNX0.XiSSigDo8Um14aFlYlRv0zUxtXGuaydaDPy09CfsD7o/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"3712\" id=\"8ab99\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%205568%203712'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"5568\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/picture-of-a-human-brain-taken-by-a-positron-emission-news-photo/1079950976?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: <a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/search/photographer?family=editorial&amp;photographer=FRED+TANNEAU\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">FRED TANNEAU</a> via Getty Images</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>We now know that the premise of Comte's worry is false. The human brain is not a single, indivisible organ. Instead, the brain is made up of billions of small components — neurons — that each crackle with electrical activity and participate in a wiring diagram of mind-boggling complexity. Out of the interactions among these cells, our entire mental life — our thoughts and feelings, hopes and dreams — flickers in and out of existence. But rather than being a meaningless tangle of connections with no discernible structure, this wiring diagram also has a broader architecture that divides the brain into distinct regions, each engaged in specialized computations. Just as a map of a city need not include individual houses to be useful, we can obtain a rough overview of how different areas of the human brain are working together at the scale of regions rather than individual brain cells. Some areas of the cortex are closer to the inputs (such as the eyes) and others are further up the processing chain. For instance, some regions are primarily involved in seeing (the visual cortex, at the back of the brain), others in processing sounds (the auditory cortex), while others are involved in storing and retrieving memories (such as the hippocampus).</p>\n<p>In a reply to Comte in 1865, the British philosopher John Stuart Mill anticipated the idea that self-awareness might also depend on the interaction of processes operating within a single brain and was thus a legitimate target of scientific study. Now, thanks to the advent of powerful brain imaging technologies such as functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI), we know that when we self-reflect, particular brain networks indeed crackle into life and that damage or disease to these same networks can lead to devastating impairments of self-awareness.</p>\n<p>I often think that if we were not so thoroughly familiar with our own capacity for self-awareness, we would be gobsmacked that the brain is able to pull off this marvelous conjuring trick. Imagine for a moment that you are a scientist on a mission to study new life-forms found on a distant planet. Biologists back on Earth are clamoring to know what they're made of and what makes them tick. But no one suggests just asking them! And yet a Martian landing on Earth, after learning a bit of English or Spanish or French, could do just that. The Martians might be stunned to find that we can already tell them something about what it is like to remember, dream, laugh, cry, or feel elated or regretful — all by virtue of being self-aware.</p>\n<blockquote>\n I find it a remarkable fact that something unique about our biology has allowed the human brain to turn its thoughts on itself.\n</blockquote>\n<p>But self-awareness did not just evolve to allow us to tell each other (and potential Martian visitors) about our thoughts and feelings. Instead, being self-aware is central to how we experience the world. We not only perceive our surroundings; we can also reflect on the beauty of a sunset, wonder whether our vision is blurred, and ask whether our senses are being fooled by illusions or magic tricks. We not only make decisions about whether to take a new job or whom to marry; we can also reflect on whether we made a good or bad choice. We not only recall childhood memories; we can also question whether these memories might be mistaken.</p>\n<p>Self-awareness also enables us to understand that other people have minds like ours. Being self-aware allows me to ask, \"How does this seem to me?\" and, equally importantly, \"How will this seem to someone else?\" Literary novels would become meaningless if we lost the ability to think about the minds of others and compare their experiences to our own. Without self-awareness, there would be no organized education. We would not know who needs to learn or whether we have the capacity to teach them. The writer Vladimir Nabokov elegantly captured this idea that self-awareness is a catalyst for human flourishing:</p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"Being aware of being aware of being. In other words, if I not only know that I am but also know that I know it, then I belong to the human species. All the rest follow s— the glory of thought, poetry, a vision of the universe. In that respect, the gap between ape and man is immeasurably greater than the one between amoeba and ape.\"</p>\n<p>In light of these myriad benefits, it's not surprising that cultivating accurate self-awareness has long been considered a wise and noble goal. In Plato's dialogue <em>Charmides</em>, Socrates has just returned from fighting in the Peloponnesian War. On his way home, he asks a local boy, Charmides, if he has worked out the meaning of sophrosyne — the Greek word for temperance or moderation, and the essence of a life well lived. After a long debate, the boy's cousin Critias suggests that the key to sophrosyne is simple: self-awareness. Socrates sums up his argument: \"Then the wise or temperate man, and he only, will know himself, and be able to examine what he knows or does not know…No other person will be able to do this.\"</p>\n<p>Likewise, the ancient Greeks were urged to \"know thyself\" by a prominent inscription carved into the stone of the Temple of Delphi. For them, self-awareness was a work in progress and something to be striven toward. This view persisted into medieval religious traditions: for instance, the Italian priest and philosopher Saint Thomas Aquinas suggested that while God knows Himself by default, we need to put in time and effort to know our own minds. Aquinas and his monks spent long hours engaged in silent contemplation. They believed that only by participating in concerted self-reflection could they ascend toward the image of God.</p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"17962c368f10e3d6db7ed0b6b37281e3\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3Nzk5NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyODgwNzQxM30.pr1K9-m0j-zHYoW7BcsvFrB0JbYzh4jKlAydTUYCjfU/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"2000\" id=\"7694f\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%203000%202000'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"3000\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/buddhist-monks-meditate-at-the-yard-of-borobudur-temple-news-photo/74377267?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: <a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/search/photographer?family=editorial&amp;photographer=Dimas+Ardian\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Dimas Ardian</a> via Getty Images</small></p>\n<p><br /></p>\n<p>A similar notion of striving toward self-awareness is seen in Eastern traditions such as Buddhism. The spiritual goal of enlightenment is to dissolve the ego, allowing more transparent and direct knowledge of our minds acting in the here and now. The founder of Chinese Taoism, Lao Tzu, captured this idea that gaining self-awareness is one of the highest pursuits when he wrote, \"To know that one does not know is best; Not to know but to believe that one knows is a disease.\"</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>Today, there is a plethora of websites, blogs, and self-help books that encourage us to \"find ourselves\" and become more self-aware. The sentiment is well meant. But while we are often urged to have better self-awareness, little attention is paid to how self-awareness actually works. I find this odd. It would be strange to encourage people to fix their cars without knowing how the engine worked, or to go to the gym without knowing which muscles to exercise. This book aims to fill this gap. I don't pretend to give pithy advice or quotes to put on a poster. Instead, I aim to provide a guide to the building blocks of self-awareness, drawing on the latest research from psychology, computer science, and neuroscience. By understanding how self-awareness works, I aim to put us in a position to answer the Athenian call to use it better.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/AGxYmLKBlmg\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 22 Apr 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3Nzk4My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDc5MzQ4OH0.9US6XRjm_6aDnzlqPHizuJdZUSe43zZLgUiC6eqH2dg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C45%2C0%2C356&height=700","linkMd5":"d1d56d14d9c26909365b722e65b15f2c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn61@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-17-451_9f12e11e90be70c3.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":60602,"destBytes":60602,"author":"Stephen Fleming","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3Nzk4My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyMDc5MzQ4OH0.9US6XRjm_6aDnzlqPHizuJdZUSe43zZLgUiC6eqH2dg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C45%2C0%2C356&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn61@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-17-451_9f12e11e90be70c3.webp","https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%205568%203712'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%203000%202000'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/AGxYmLKBlmg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn100@2020_6/2021/04/23/17-38-18-397_0854d1cdd85584a7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333837},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Brain mapping: explained","link":"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/brain-mapping","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NDU4NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MTM5MzMzM30.TR3eYwlW-XRV4nj3RZl6f1Gskst5Eco2kNmAH-E-XR8/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>Brain mapping is an attempt to identify the location of everything in the brain.</li>\n <li><span></span>An accurate map of the brain would immeasurably enhance our ability to understand how it works.</li>\n <li>The project is massive, involving multiple fields of biomedical research and expensive cutting-edge technology.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p> Brain mapping is one of the hottest current areas of research. <br /> </p>\n<p> The brain is nothing short of amazing. Billions of <a href=\"https://qbi.uq.edu.au/brain/brain-anatomy/what-neuron\" target=\"_blank\">neurons</a> are in there — the current best guess is about <a href=\"https://centerforinquiry.org/blog/how-many-neurons-are-in-the-human-brain-a-journey-to-find-the-answer/\" target=\"_blank\">86 billion</a> — and a roughly equal number of non-neuronal cells. The number of interconnections, or synapses, across which neurons communicate via chemical and electrical signals is believed to be about <a href=\"https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2010/11/101117121803.htm\" target=\"_blank\">125 trillion</a>. There's a whole universe in there, even though the <a href=\"https://faculty.washington.edu/chudler/ffacts.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">average adult brain</a> weighs merely three pounds and measures just 140 mm x 167 mm x 93 mm.</p>\n<p> Though we know a lot about the anatomy of the brain, its functions remain largely enigmatic. For instance, what is the biological mechanism that encodes memories? On a computer, files are encoded digitally with a series of ones and zeroes, a type of discrete storage. Cassette tapes are analog recordings, and information is stored magnetically. How does the brain store information? <a href=\"https://www.technologyreview.com/2018/05/21/142804/does-the-brain-store-information-in-discrete-or-analog-form/\" target=\"_blank\">We don't know</a>. Where consciousness is located in the brain — that is, the parts and functions that make us \"us\" — is likewise shrouded in mystery. </p>\n<p> The challenge is described well by the journal <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/news/the-benefits-of-brain-mapping-1.13386\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Nature</em></a>: </p>\n<p rel=\"margin-left: 20px;\" style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"> <em>\"Neuroscientists know frighteningly little about the brain's complexity. They have sketched out the broad anatomy of the brain, and realize that individual functions… are mediated by circuitry that crosses anatomical borders. They can examine the detailed electrical activity of small numbers of neurons. They can wield imaging technologies that show which brain areas are activated during defined tasks, such as viewing pleasant or unpleasant pictures. But those tiny (in brain terms) pieces of information have not led neuroscientists to the big picture: what we mean by human consciousness, what makes us our individual selves or why some people develop psychiatric disorders. Neuroscientists need to be able to join the dots — and there are a lot of dots.\"</em> </p>\n<p> As intimidating as this is, neuroscience is making incremental progress. We can correlate various actions and thoughts with brain activity. Scientists at Berkeley, for example, can tell what part of your brain will exhibit electrical activity <a href=\"https://news.berkeley.edu/2019/08/19/readingbrainmap/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">when you read certain words and phrases</a>. </p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-youtube\"> <span class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"62048301eecc1e967d02a1a1d9741a52\" style=\"display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;\"><iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"auto\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/k61nJkx5aDQ?rel=0\" style=\"position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;\" width=\"100%\"></iframe></span> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UC7c8mE90qCtu11z47U0KErg\" target=\"_blank\">Nature</a></small> </p>\n<h2>Two types of \"brain mapping\"</h2>\n<p> Before we dive further into the field of brain mapping, let's first define what we're talking about. There are actually two types of brain mapping. <br /> </p>\n<p> The first type, which is what we are concerned with, is described by the <a href=\"https://www.worldbrainmapping.org/About/What-Is-Brain-Mapping\" target=\"_blank\">Society for Brain Mapping &amp; Therapeutics</a> as \"the study of the anatomy and function of the brain and spinal cord through the use of imaging, immunohistochemistry, molecular and optogenetics, stem cell and cellular biology, engineering, neurophysiology, and nanotechnology.\" One might fairly add physics and quantum physics to that list. </p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img alt=\"brain mapping\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"4b6b9fdf78276fbfc7becb64615a38c6\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NDU5OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyODY1Mjc2Nn0.WId0JQ_SW6BVFFycULbqn6Q_QkkUehk7UyXc3alQ2fk/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"467\" id=\"bfebc\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20467'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1440\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://stock.adobe.com/contributor/203447130/santiago-silver?load_type=author&amp;prev_url=detail\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">santiago silver</a> / Adobe Stock / Big Think</small> </p>\n<div style=\"display: none;\"> \n <img alt=\"brain mapping\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"4b6b9fdf78276fbfc7becb64615a38c6\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NDU5OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyODY1Mjc2Nn0.WId0JQ_SW6BVFFycULbqn6Q_QkkUehk7UyXc3alQ2fk/img.jpg?width=980\" width=\"1440\" /> \n</div>\n<div style=\"display: none;\"> \n <img alt=\"brain mapping\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"4b6b9fdf78276fbfc7becb64615a38c6\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NDU5OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyODY1Mjc2Nn0.WId0JQ_SW6BVFFycULbqn6Q_QkkUehk7UyXc3alQ2fk/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"467\" id=\"bfebc\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20467'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1440\" /> \n</div>\n<p> The second type of brain mapping deals with identifying areas of the brain using <a href=\"https://nhahealth.com/brain-mapping/\" target=\"_blank\">qEEG</a> technology in order to strengthen or heal them through <a href=\"https://www.psychologytoday.com/us/therapy-types/neurofeedback\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">neurofeedback</a> training. Neurofeedback practitioners claim some impressive therapeutic value for people with all sorts of conditions relating to the brain, including <a href=\"https://www.additudemag.com/neurofeedback-adhd-brain-training/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">ADHD</a>, <a href=\"https://www.mysuncoast.com/2020/02/13/next-decade-healthcare-brain-mapping-autism/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">autism</a>, <a href=\"https://www.centerforbrain.com/how-brain-mapping-helps-target-anxiety-depression-and-adhd-with-neurofeedback/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">depression, and anxiety</a>. Some experts have expressed skepticism about some such claims. The <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4892319/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">jury's still out</a> on this type of brain mapping. </p>\n<h2>What kind of map could map the brain?</h2>\n<p> A brain map, therefore, could be something like an atlas — a collection of maps that document various neural pathways. But, unlike a road map, it can't be two-dimensional. A brain map of the cortex alone would have to be three-dimensional. </p>\n<blockquote>\n The number of interconnections, or synapses, across which neurons communicate via chemical and electrical signals is believed to be about \n <a href=\"https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2010/11/101117121803.htm\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">125 trillion</a>. \n</blockquote>\n<p> The cortex, or gray matter, which contains billions of neurons and synapses is <a href=\"https://www.livescience.com/62892-why-brains-have-folds.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">folded</a> in such a way that sections that would be distant from each other come into close proximity. This is useful because it shortens the distance that signals have to cross from one part of the brain to another. The folds also greatly increase the cortex's surface area, which means we can cram more gray matter inside our skulls. </p>\n<p> Folding itself is implicated in some neural disorders, and scientists wonder if we might one day be able to modify a brain's folding. </p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-youtube\"> <span class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"6b6298aa458f19c5efd05eacd5b423d5\" style=\"display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;\"><iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"auto\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/x9IXtTbt2f0?rel=0\" style=\"position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;\" width=\"100%\"></iframe></span> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCUL-pmhmDcZDwsA4cX2HO5w\" target=\"_blank\">PhD Comics</a></small> </p>\n<h2>A need for unprecedented collaboration<br /> </h2>\n<p> Aside from the obvious scientific and technological difficulties, <a href=\"https://www.martinlamonica.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Martin La Monica</a>, writing for <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/three-reasons-to-think-twice-about-mapping-the-brain-8034\" target=\"_blank\">The Conversation</a>, wonders if some human barriers may also get in the way. He raises three concerns:<br /> </p>\n<ol class=\"ee-ol\"> \n <li><strong>Maps have always betrayed the bias of their creators.</strong> Even neural cartographers will inevitably develop maps that depict the brain according to their understanding of its workings. At the same time, it's exciting to imagine breakthroughs that could occur should a map unexpectedly not conform to its makers expectations.</li> \n <li><strong>One size does not fit all.</strong> Scientists strongly suspect each brain is at least somewhat unique. To construct brain maps that encompass differences between us, researchers will have to engage in some generalizing that will inevitably reduce their accuracy as it enhances their universality.</li> \n <li><strong>Financial considerations make the requisite collaboration between scientists and institutions difficult. </strong>The hardware and expertise required mean that brain mapping will be costly. However, for those who discover new medical treatments or technologies along the way, the endeavor could prove profitable. Thus, some will no doubt feel that they have financial incentives not to share information.</li> \n</ol>\n<p> Ultimately, La Monica's third consideration touches upon what may be the human brain mapping's biggest underlying challenge. As <a href=\"https://www.uclahealth.org/neurology/brain-mapping\" target=\"_blank\">UCLA Health</a> notes, the project is the polar opposite of \"reductionistic approaches in medical science.\" Instead, \"brain mapping integrates many sources of information to produce a holistic view, the value of which is greater than the sum of its parts.\" </p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img alt=\"brain mapping\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"27277cabe351c5283958730268136a95\" href=\"https://stock.adobe.com/contributor/206290892/gerasimov174?load_type=author&amp;prev_url=detail\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">gerasimov174</a> / Adobe Stock</small> </p>\n<div style=\"display: none;\">\n <img alt=\"brain mapping\" class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"7763ebe8fcfce7d695e03eabca4d6acf\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" width=\"1440\" />\n</div>\n<p> This will demand an unprecedented level of collaboration and cooperation between organizations and scientists from a broad swath of scientific disciplines. </p>\n<h2>Brain mapping for the win</h2>\n<p> There is almost nothing about mapping the human brain that will be easy. From logistical issues (like the open exchange of information) to scientific challenges (such as technological and theoretical advances), much will be required to make sense of the human brain. </p>\n<p> With the brain so central to our being, there's a tremendous amount of research relating to it. There's a continual stream of new insights regarding the way it functions and the ways it sometimes <em>doesn't</em> function so well. </p>\n<p> <span></span>For scientists seeking to understand the brain, and for doctors working to help their patients enjoy life to its fullest, a comprehensive map that brings all of the best, most recent information together is more than worth the Herculean effort required to make it happen. </p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/sYaFfwigOds\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 19 Apr 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NDU4NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MTM5MzMzM30.TR3eYwlW-XRV4nj3RZl6f1Gskst5Eco2kNmAH-E-XR8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"69792499b49712a60f4fc1d814c68f30","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn95@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-394_8775a2f098db964f.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":33260,"destBytes":33260,"author":"Robby Berman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NDU4NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MTM5MzMzM30.TR3eYwlW-XRV4nj3RZl6f1Gskst5Eco2kNmAH-E-XR8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn95@2020_4/2021/04/23/17-38-17-394_8775a2f098db964f.webp","https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20467'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201440%20900'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/sYaFfwigOds":null},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333838},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Brain-controlled chess is here","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/brain-controlled-chess","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzUxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDcyMTA5OH0.q_3-IYhPjhXmKxmCdOSesgBxlmxkeHkbODGM1qQ5WKI/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C47%2C0%2C161&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>A brain-controlled interface implements a two-step process: Identify the chess piece, then place it on the board.</li>\n <li><span></span>The program was 96 percent accurate at correctly moving chess pieces.</li>\n <li>This research opens up opportunities for physically impaired people to express themselves in new ways.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>By November 2020, <em>The Queen's Gambit</em> had been watched in over 62 million households, making it the <a href=\"https://variety.com/2020/digital/news/queens-gambit-netflix-viewing-record-1234838090/\" target=\"_blank\">most-watched scripted limited series</a> in Netflix history. The thoughtful, stylized show on the patient pursuit of chess was perfectly timed for a world in lockdown. Viewers were enamored with Elizabeth Harmon's mental prowess as she envisioned dozens of potential moves on the ceiling in order to evade capture and declare victory.</p>\n<p>Chess is one of the most intellectually stimulating games in existence. What if you could play it purely with your mind? Given how immersive chess is, can you imagine leaving the physical world behind and truly entering a mind palace? </p>\n<h2>A game of the mind</h2>\n<p>It turns out a number of researchers have had such a vision. In a paper published in <a href=\"https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/8856965\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>IEEE Xplore</em></a>, German professors David Hubner, Albrecht Schall, and Michael Tangermann discuss the evolution of brain-computer interfaces (BCIs) as applied to two-player chess games. Incredibly, 96 percent of moves were correct — and most errors were technical issues that they believe can be fixed. </p>\n<p>Chess is played by an estimated <a href=\"https://www.chess.com/news/view/how-popular-is-chess-8306\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">605 million people</a> around the world. While face-to-face interactions will always be an integral component of the intimacy of chess, the game was adopted early on the internet. The ability to play at a distance increased its popularity and accessibility as players found suitable opponents across the planet.</p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image image-crop-16x9\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"df90f15eca6deebfa4e4c92e1240d8c9\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzUxOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1OTQ1ODkwN30.GlT6Vbi0_iKjeDBlQgh_1YwZYD9DyGmzKjV3dojcqPw/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C732%2C0%2C205&amp;height=700\" height=\"700\" id=\"673f9\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201245%20700'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1245\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Photo: Anusorn / Adobe Stock</small></p>\n<p>The BCI is based on a two-step process: first, identifying the piece a player wants to move, then moving it on the board. In this study, six players used a BCI chess application (which was based on an open-source Java app) along with an electroencephalogram (EEG) equipped with 31 passive electrodes that detected the chess piece and board position in the player's mind.<br /></p>\n<p>Before the game, each player performed predefined chess moves to calibrate the BCI. During play, they also had a predefined amount of time for thinking about their next move. Specifically, they were given 15 seconds to consider the piece that they were going to move and five seconds to \"move\" the piece. If the player only had one possible move, the BCI automatically executed it. </p>\n<h2>Hands-free chess has real-world applications</h2>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>Beyond the excitement of controlling a computer with your mind, the researchers recognize a variety of potential applications. For instance, BCI games aid in cognitive training and help motor-impaired people express themselves. </p>\n<p>--</p>\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p><em>Stay in touch with Derek on <a href=\"http://www.twitter.com/derekberes\" target=\"_blank\">Twitter</a> and <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/DerekBeresdotcom\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Facebook</a>. His most recent book is</em> \"<em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B08KRVMP2M?pf_rd_r=MDJW43337675SZ0X00FH&amp;pf_rd_p=edaba0ee-c2fe-4124-9f5d-b31d6b1bfbee\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Hero's Dose: The Case For Psychedelics in Ritual and Therapy</a>.\"</em></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Bwe9VOP2AcA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 15 Apr 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzUxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDcyMTA5OH0.q_3-IYhPjhXmKxmCdOSesgBxlmxkeHkbODGM1qQ5WKI/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C47%2C0%2C161&height=700","linkMd5":"f0a6c4c461877424f510934b75d044ca","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn35@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-519_1b176628bbeaec9a.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":86064,"destBytes":86064,"author":"Derek Beres","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1MzUxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDcyMTA5OH0.q_3-IYhPjhXmKxmCdOSesgBxlmxkeHkbODGM1qQ5WKI/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C47%2C0%2C161&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn35@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-519_1b176628bbeaec9a.webp","https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201245%20700'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Bwe9VOP2AcA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn56@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-18-422_679b71c29de6c964.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333838},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"How the Yazoo Land Scandal changed American history","link":"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/how-the-yazoo-land-scandal-changed-american-history","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1ODgxNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTQzMjA2M30.gVGZSS73FZMoRCrvgR0jrybyEEHwbklVM2Kk0aSRx_M/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" /> \n<br /> \n<br /> \n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Few people today are familiar with the Yazoo Land Scandal, which broke in the mid-1790s.</li> \n <li>Yet it sent shockwaves through American public life, influencing politics, law, and even geography.</li> \n <li>Without it, Georgia could have been a \"super state\" — and the Trail of Tears might not have happened. </li> \n</ul> \n<hr /> \n<p><strong><br /></strong></p> \n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d5a437f757ef4ac5c80e4de669dff590\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1ODgyNS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NjU1NjA3MX0.ZU0jDNyj8EHaCsk1M2A1PxJFMcgeOGh-8wFudRfesho/img.png?width=980\" height=\"1240\" id=\"4ef10\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201915%201240'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1915\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">Seven of the original 13 states had extensive territorial claims, mainly toward the west.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.loc.gov/resource/g3701sm.gct00077/?sp=34&amp;r=-1.353,-0.114,3.707,1.549,0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Library of Congress</a> via public domain</small></p> \n<p>There are no good old days.<br /></p> \n<p>Travel back, say, to the presidency of George Washington himself. Yes, the father of the nation, he who could tell no lie. Even under POTUS #1, there was corruption so venal and egregious that it changed the very map of America. In other words, without the Yazoo Land Scandal, the political geography of the United States might have looked quite different. Yet despite its catchy name and far-reaching consequences, few now remember the affair.</p> \n<p>The scandal centered on Georgia, the last holdout in the process of state cessions. Of the original 13 colonies-turned-states, seven had entered into the Union with vague, contested, and often overlapping land claims, mainly in the region between the Appalachian Mountains and the Mississippi River.</p> \n<p>The six states without claims did not want to be overshadowed by their expanding neighbors. And the federal government did not want them to get into fights where their claims overlapped. So the U.S. government spent its first few years convincing and cajoling those seven states to abandon their claims. When New York relinquished its claim to Vermont in 1790 (for a mere $30,000), that process was complete. With one exception.</p> \n<p><strong>Yazoo Land Fraud</strong></p> \n<p>Georgia continued to claim territory all the way to the Mississippi River. For various reasons, the state was loath to give up its interest in these so-called Yazoo Lands, corresponding to the larger part of the present-day states of Mississippi and Alabama. Not least because of money. Land developers were eager to acquire large chunks of the country, their guiding principle being: bribe high, pay low.</p> \n<p>In 1794, four companies, set up especially for the purpose, paid half a million dollars for about 40 million acres of land. Even taking into account all the bribes — another half a million — that was a ridiculously low amount: four acres to a dollar.</p> \n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"8a60b452782c82cb9c441e6fbdadcf33\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1ODgzMC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYxOTMyNzgxOX0.yC0LtpqFy2UZqdWgMzo25v8NXcwRH2pLQinLldLX9xI/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"600\" id=\"13933\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20800%20600'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"800\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">Following the Rescinding Act, Georgia's governor and legislators burn all copies of the Yazoo Act (except one).</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.georgiaencyclopedia.org/articles/history-archaeology/yazoo-land-fraud\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">New Georgia Encyclopedia</a> via public domain</small></p> \n<p>Infuriated by the deal, Georgians booted out the legislators who had their palms greased to approve the Yazoo Act, by which Georgia had sold all that land on the cheap. In 1795, a new state legislature voted a Rescinding Act, overturning the sale. All extant copies of the original Act were collected and burned at high noon on the grounds of the state capitol under construction, then in Louisville. (One copy escaped destruction — the one sent to President Washington).<br /></p> \n<p><strong>The Yazoo hits the fan</strong></p> \n<p>But that was far from the end of the unpleasantries. In fact, this is where the actual scandal started. For the land companies did not admit defeat. They continued printing bonds that were being traded and sold on the financial markets of New York, Boston, and Philadelphia, raking in tidy profits.</p> \n<p>Thousands of bond buyers acquired a stake in the Yazoo Lands. Eventually, though, the market smelled a rat. Investors started to worry: had they thrown away their money on a fraudulent land scheme?</p> \n<p>Georgia paid back some of the duped buyers, but unable to handle the escalating scale of the scandal, the state eventually did surrender its claims to the Yazoo Lands to the federal government. Under the so-called Compact of 1802, the U.S. paid Georgia $1.25 million, took over any remaining liability for the Yazoo Lands, and promised to rid Georgia of any remaining Native American land claims.</p> \n<p>So, the duped investors could now sue the federal government instead of Georgia. The land companies, for their part, wanted the U.S. to uphold their claims, which they continued to consider legal and valid. Who was right?</p> \n<p>In 1810, the case reached the highest court in the land. Pronouncing on <em>Fletcher v. Peck</em>, the Supreme Court ruled that the Rescinding Act was unconstitutional and the original land deals remained legal. For although those deals were corrupt and not in the best interest of Georgians, the contracts were made by the Georgia legislature, which had the authority to do so. The Supreme Court ordered the U.S. government to pay out $4.5 million in compensation to the claimants.</p> \n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"b604eeefe5c47a246cc9d6c8ece149e9\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1ODgzMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTcwMjgyMH0.dd9D9-GOMW4dPkRqe7Pc8_SACtOt2wrU4bNYFs67_vQ/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"1461\" id=\"2412b\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" src=\"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201208%201461'%3E%3C/svg%3E\" width=\"1208\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">Overview of the four separate Yazoo Act land deals that together constitute 40 million acres of land, sold for just $1 million (including bribes).</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.georgiaencyclopedia.org/articles/history-archaeology/yazoo-land-fraud\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">New Georgia Encyclopedia</a> via public domain</small></p> \n<p><strong>Yazoo changed the course of American history</strong><br /></p> \n<p><em>Fletcher v. Peck</em> was a landmark case in more ways than one. For the first time ever, the Supreme Court had ruled against a state law, that is, Georgia's Rescinding Act. This established the principle that federal laws were supreme over state laws. The case also firmly established that a legal contract could not be nullified by a later law, which became an important principle in contract law.</p> \n<p>The Yazoo Land Scandal had two further, major consequences for the United States. Without the scandal, Georgia might conceivably have managed to hold on to its western lands. This hypothetical Greater Georgia, running from the Atlantic to the Mississippi, would have comprised most or all of the current states of Mississippi and Alabama. That would make it one of America's most populous states, its 20 million inhabitants on par with Florida and New York and surpassed only by Texas (30 million) and California (40 million).</p> \n<p>Georgia could also have avoided one of the most ignominious events in its history. In 1830, the federal government fulfilled its promise in the Compact of 1802 to rid Georgia of all extant Native American land claims by the Indian Removal Act. Signed into law by President Andrew Jackson, the Act led to the \"Trail of Tears,\" the forcible removal of the Cherokee, Creek, Choctaw, Seminole, and Chickasaw tribes — about 100,000 people in all — to reservations west of the Mississippi, in what would later become Oklahoma.</p> \n<p>Although now largely forgotten, the Yazoo Land Scandal helped shape the territory, laws, and institutions of the early United States. But the affair has another lesson for our times. If there are no good old days, then our current ones perhaps aren't so bad either.</p> \n<p><strong>Strange Maps #1079</strong></p> \n<p><em>Got a strange map? Let me know at <a href=\"mailto:strangemaps@gmail.com\">strangemaps@gmail.com</a>.</em></p> \n<p><em><em>Follow Strange Maps on <a href=\"https://twitter.com/FrankJacobs\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Twitter</a> and on <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/VeryStrangeMaps\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Facebook</a>.</em></em> <br /></p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/-R7JD3I0yHE\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 19 Apr 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1ODgxNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTQzMjA2M30.gVGZSS73FZMoRCrvgR0jrybyEEHwbklVM2Kk0aSRx_M/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"5a8e356afda15396bf9255eb7f6b6ceb","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn60@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-453_4a5c4ddf1d9421bd.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":125224,"destBytes":125224,"author":"Frank Jacobs","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA1ODgxNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMTQzMjA2M30.gVGZSS73FZMoRCrvgR0jrybyEEHwbklVM2Kk0aSRx_M/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn60@2020_5/2021/04/23/17-38-17-453_4a5c4ddf1d9421bd.webp","https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201915%201240'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20800%20600'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201208%201461'%3E%3C/svg%3E":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/-R7JD3I0yHE":null},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333837},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"A historian identifies the worst year in human history","link":"https://bigthink.com/politics-current-affairs/worst-year-in-human-history","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NTg1MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjA2NTc4NX0.qWUn5KHK8kZnNgDxfZHzemCpk0-YGPoFNQ1By0PsvoM/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C114%2C0%2C114&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\">\n <li>Harvard professor Michael McCormick argues the worst year to be alive was 536 AD.</li>\n <li><span></span>The year was terrible due to cataclysmic eruptions that blocked out the sun and the spread of the plague.</li>\n <li>536 ushered in the coldest decade in thousands of years and started a century of economic devastation.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<p>The past year has been nothing but the worst in the lives of many people around the globe. A rampaging pandemic, dangerous political instability, weather catastrophes, and a profound change in lifestyle that most have never experienced or imagined. </p>\n<p>But was it the worst year ever? </p>\n<p>Nope. Not even close. In the eyes of the historian and archaeologist Michael McCormick, the absolute \"worst year to be alive\" was 536.</p>\n<p>Why was 536 so bad? You could certainly argue that 1918, the last year of World War I when the Spanish Flu killed up to 100 million people around the world, was a terrible year by all accounts. 1349 could also be considered on this morbid list as the year when the Black Death wiped out half of Europe, with up to 20 million dead from the plague. Most of the years of World War II could probably lay claim to the \"worst year\" title as well. But 536 was in a category of its own, argues the historian.</p>\n<p><strong>It all began with an eruption...</strong></p>\n<p>According to <a href=\"https://history.fas.harvard.edu/people/michael-mccormick\" target=\"_blank\">McCormick</a>, Professor of Medieval History at Harvard University, 536 was the precursor year to one of the worst periods of human history. It featured a volcanic eruption early in the year that took place in Iceland, as established by a study of a Swiss glacier carried out by McCormick and the glaciologist Paul Mayewski from the Climate Change Institute of The University of Maine (UM) in Orono.</p>\n<p>The ash spewed out by the volcano likely led to a fog that brought an 18-month-long stretch of daytime darkness across Europe, the Middle East, and portions of Asia. As <a href=\"https://www.sciencemag.org/news/2018/11/why-536-was-worst-year-be-alive\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">wrote</a> the Byzantine historian Procopius, \"For the sun gave forth its light without brightness, like the moon, during the whole year.\" He also recounted that it looked like the sun was always in eclipse.</p>\n<p>Cassiodorus, a Roman politician of that time, <a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?id=LRhwDwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA392&amp;lpg=PA392&amp;dq=%22we+marvel+to+see+no+shadows+of+our+bodies+at+noon%22&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=f2r9-3sIC0&amp;sig=sqedxRKc7JepUErZJH8doFJ5jRE&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=2ahUKEwjGiaCQufreAhVJh-AKHS_vDhwQ6AEwAHoECAUQAQ#v=onepage&amp;q=%22we%20marvel%20to%20see%20no%20shadows%20of%20our%20bodies%20at%20noon%22&amp;f=false\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">wrote</a> that the sun had a \"bluish\" color, the moon had no luster, and \"seasons seem to be all jumbled up together.\" What's even creepier, he described, \"We marvel to see no shadows of our bodies at noon.\"</p>\n<p><strong>...that led to famine...</strong></p>\n<p>The dark days also brought a period of coldness, with summer temperatures falling by 1.5° C. to 2.5° C. This started the coldest decade in the past 2300 years, <a href=\"https://www.sciencemag.org/news/2018/11/why-536-was-worst-year-be-alive\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">reports</a> <em>Science</em>, leading to the devastation of crops and worldwide hunger.</p>\n<p><strong>...and the fall of an empire</strong></p>\n<p>In 541, the bubonic plague added considerably to the world's misery. Spreading from the Roman port of Pelusium in Egypt, the so-called Plague of Justinian caused the deaths of up to one half of the population of the eastern Roman Empire. This, in turn, sped up its eventual collapse, writes McCormick.</p>\n<p>Between the environmental cataclysms, with massive volcanic eruptions also in 540 and 547, and the devastation brought on by the plague, Europe was in for an economic downturn for nearly all of the next century, until 640 when silver mining gave it a boost.</p>\n<p><strong>Was that the worst time in history?</strong> </p>\n<p>Of course, the absolute worst time in history depends on who you were and where you lived. </p>\n<p>Native Americans can easily point to 1520, when smallpox, brought over by the Spanish, killed millions of indigenous people. By 1600, up to 90 percent of the population of the Americas (about 55 million people) was wiped out by various European pathogens.</p>\n<p>Like all things, the grisly title of \"worst year ever\" comes down to historical perspective.</p>\n<hr />\n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/gERNGeOh4ls\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 20 Apr 2021 22:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NTg1MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjA2NTc4NX0.qWUn5KHK8kZnNgDxfZHzemCpk0-YGPoFNQ1By0PsvoM/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C114%2C0%2C114&height=700","linkMd5":"77ba87a8f146b4ef4365dbb740dd93fa","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn63@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-491_a32abb8d5af80f9b.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":214942,"destBytes":214942,"author":"Paul Ratner","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA3NTg1MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjA2NTc4NX0.qWUn5KHK8kZnNgDxfZHzemCpk0-YGPoFNQ1By0PsvoM/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C114%2C0%2C114&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn63@2020_2/2021/04/23/17-38-17-491_a32abb8d5af80f9b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/gERNGeOh4ls":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn45@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-18-391_506016d17cdc8eeb.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333833},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"Smartly dressed: Researchers develop clothes that sense movement via touch","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/smart-clothing","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NTE0MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTM1NjM1NH0.EhAEm9DlQEcUvq_37LpS4c-QAZPcrS1c8vIV1WM53FU/img.jpg?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C254%2C0%2C255&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<p>In recent years there have been exciting breakthroughs in wearable technologies, like smartwatches that can monitor your breathing and blood oxygen levels. </p>\n<hr />\n<p>But what about a wearable that can detect how you move as you do a physical activity or play a sport, and could potentially even offer feedback on how to improve your technique? </p>\n<p>And, as a major bonus, what if the wearable were something you'd actually already be wearing, like a shirt of a pair of socks?</p>\n<p>That's the idea behind a new set of MIT-designed clothing that use special fibers to sense a person's movement via touch. Among other things, the researchers showed that their clothes can actually <a href=\"https://www.dropbox.com/sh/0hoh7coh5wx3w9e/AABt4A08vFyty4j9_hBrQ6j-a?dl=0&amp;preview=SupplementaryVideo5.mp4\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">determine things like if someone is sitting, walking, or doing particular poses</a>.</p>\n<p>The group from MIT's Computer Science and Artificial Intelligence Lab (CSAIL) says that their clothes could be used for athletic training and rehabilitation. With patients' permission, they could even help passively monitor the health of residents in assisted-care facilities and determine if, for example, someone has fallen or is unconscious. <em> </em></p>\n<p>The researchers have developed a range of prototypes, from socks and gloves to a full vest. The team's \"tactile electronics\" use a mix of more typical textile fibers alongside a small amount of custom-made functional fibers that sense pressure from the person wearing the garment.</p>\n<p>According to CSAIL graduate student Yiyue Luo, a key advantage of the team's design is that, unlike many existing wearable electronics, theirs can be incorporated into traditional large-scale clothing production. The machine-knitted tactile textiles are soft, stretchable, breathable, and can take a wide range of forms. </p>\n<p>\"Traditionally it's been hard to develop a mass-production wearable that provides high-accuracy data across a large number of sensors,\" says Luo, lead author on a new paper about the project that is appearing in this month's edition of <em>Nature Electronics</em>. \"When you manufacture lots of sensor arrays, some of them will not work and some of them will work worse than others, so we developed a self-correcting mechanism that uses a self-supervised machine learning algorithm to recognize and adjust when certain sensors in the design are off-base.\"</p>\n<p>The team's clothes have a range of capabilities. Their socks predict motion by looking at how different sequences of tactile footprints correlate to different poses as the user transitions from one pose to another. The full-sized vest can also detect the wearers' pose, activity, and the texture of the contacted surfaces.</p>\n<p>The authors imagine a coach using the sensor to analyze people's postures and give suggestions on improvement. It could also be used by an experienced athlete to record their posture so that beginners can learn from them. In the long term, they even imagine that robots could be trained to learn how to do different activities using data from the wearables. </p>\n<p>\"Imagine robots that are no longer tactilely blind, and that have 'skins' that can provide tactile sensing just like we have as humans,\" says corresponding author Wan Shou, a postdoc at CSAIL. \"Clothing with high-resolution tactile sensing opens up a lot of exciting new application areas for researchers to explore in the years to come.\"</p>\n<p>The paper was co-written by MIT professors Antonio Torralba, Wojciech Matusik, and Tomás Palacios, alongside PhD students Yunzhu Li, Pratyusha Sharma, and Beichen Li; postdoc Kui Wu; and research engineer Michael Foshey. </p>\n<p>The work was partially funded by Toyota Research Institute.</p>\n<p>Reprinted with permission of <a href=\"http://news.mit.edu/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">MIT News</a>. Read the <a href=\"https://news.mit.edu/2021/tactile-textiles-sense-movement-via-touch-0331\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">original article</a>.<br /></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/8Xui1tdME_o\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 17 Apr 2021 07:06:59 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NTE0MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTM1NjM1NH0.EhAEm9DlQEcUvq_37LpS4c-QAZPcrS1c8vIV1WM53FU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C254%2C0%2C255&height=700","linkMd5":"386272f1b835d062a91938fbfeb4bc2a","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":72090,"destBytes":72090,"author":"Adam Conner-Simons | CSAIL","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA2NTE0MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTM1NjM1NH0.EhAEm9DlQEcUvq_37LpS4c-QAZPcrS1c8vIV1WM53FU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C254%2C0%2C255&height=700":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/8Xui1tdME_o":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn30@2020_3/2021/04/23/17-38-18-191_fead54b4d4b88f4c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1619199333833},{"createdTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","updatedTime":"2021-04-24 01:35:33","title":"How Europe will beat China on batteries","link":"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/gigafactories-in-europe","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4NTgwOS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MjI3OTA5Nn0.u80nPLeWWxbgoLKNtuVxLLWVC90I5DPyge2LH3Yflsk/img.png?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" /> \n<br /> \n<br /> \n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>China produces 80 percent of electric vehicle batteries.</li> \n <li>To achieve battery independence, Europe is ramping up production.</li> \n <li>And the U.S.? Action is needed, and quick.</li> \n</ul> \n<hr /> \n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"fb7b6560756cf507bd5394a2d179166d\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4NTg3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyODA2OTYzOX0.7YdbaaaUIqCvb10L2LFzJ7oaHHRAf0MWrIvzb-TPx4w/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"2250\" id=\"10b93\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" width=\"4000\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Tesla's Gigafactory near Berlin, still under construction in October last year.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://www.la-wolf.de/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Michael Wolf</a>, <a href=\"https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Tesla_Gigafactory_4_DJI_0221.jpg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-SA 3.0</a> </small></p> \n<p>This is a map of the future — the future of battery cell production in Europe. If and when all projects on this map are up and running, Europe will have a battery cell production capacity of around 700 gigawatt hours (GWh). That's crucial for two reasons: (1) those battery cells will power the electric vehicles (EVs) that will soon replace our fossil-fuel cars; and (2) a production capacity of that magnitude would break China's current near-monopoly.<br /></p> \n<p><span></span>Say what you will about state-run economies, but they're great at concentrating effort on a particular target. About a decade ago, Beijing directed huge resources towards its photovoltaic industry. Today, <a href=\"http://solarindustryforum.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Izumi_PVIndustryTrends_final.pdf\" target=\"_blank\">nine of the world's 10 largest solar panel manufacturers are at least partly Chinese</a>. China is similarly resolved to become the global leader in EVs, including EV battery production.</p> \n<p><span></span>And so far, it's working. At present, about 80% of the world's lithium-ion battery cells are made in China. Lithium-ion batteries are the ones used in EVs. In sufficient numbers, lithium-ion batteries can also be used for large-scale energy storage, which would help even out power supply fluctuations from sources like solar and wind.</p> \n<p><span></span>China's dominance in this area is making many outside China nervous. In previous decades, OPEC had a similar stranglehold on producing the oil that makes cars run and factories hum. Then the organization had a political point to make and turned off the tap. During the oil crisis of the 1970s, oil prices skyrocketed and economies crashed.</p> \n<p><strong><span></span>Battery wars</strong></p> \n<p>Avoiding a 21st-century version of that scenario requires a strategy for EV battery self-sufficiency, and Europe has one. In 2018, the EU launched its Battery Action Plan, a concerted effort to increase its battery production capacity. Realizing they couldn't beat China on price, the Europeans resolved that their batteries would be greener and more efficient.</p> \n<p>Easier said than done. Setting up battery production is complex, expensive, and slow. And as the EU's woefully slow vaccine rollout demonstrates, the organization's strength-in-numbers argument doesn't always work in its favor. Indeed, by 2020, only four of the dots on this map were up and running: </p> \n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>a facility by Envision AESC in Sunderland (UK - now ex EU)</li> \n <li>a Samsung factory in Göd (Hungary)</li> \n <li>an LG Energy Solution plant in Wroclaw (Poland)</li> \n <li>a factory by Leclanché in Willstätt (Germany)</li> \n</ul> \n<p>But in this case, slow and steady may win the race. At least two dozen battery plants are in the works across Europe (i.e. EU and its near abroad), and four of those should come online in 2021 alone, including Tesla's plant near Berlin. Tesla, incidentally, coined the term \"gigafactory\" for its facility in Sparks, Nevada. As the title of this map suggests, it's becoming the generic description for any large battery cell production facility. </p> \n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-lazyloadable-image rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"4a44f6a35d77c5362f0eba89e35122d2\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" data-runner-src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNjA4NTkxNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNTU2MzcxNX0.b6KXBpWdKmNQ66t2374Ng8oiZSpNYtHUMBTylZn1iUQ/img.jpg?width=980\" height=\"3316\" id=\"43d1e\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" width=\"4096\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">By the end of the decade, Europe will have around 30 gigafactories.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://cicenergigune.com/en\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CIC energiGUNE</a></small></p> \n<p>Despite the fact that Tesla's Nevada plant is on its way to becoming <a href=\"https://www.cnbc.com/2019/05/03/we-went-inside-teslas-gigafactory-heres-what-it-looked-like.html\" target=\"_blank\">the world's largest building</a>, battery production capacity is growing fastest in Europe. Predictions vary, but all observers agree that Europe is on the verge of a Great Leap Forward. Here's why:<br /></p> \n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Europe's current production capacity is about 30 GWh.</li> \n <li>One forecast puts that figure at 300 GWh by 2029, another even at 400 GWh by 2025.</li> \n <li>Adding up the maximum capacity of all facilities on this map comes close to 700 GWh by 2028.</li> \n <li>In terms of global capacity, BloombergNEF predicts Europe's share could increase from 7% now to 31% in 2030. </li> \n <li>According to <a href=\"https://www.eurobat.org/\" target=\"_blank\">Eurobat</a> — disappointingly, not the Gauloises-smoking, Nietzsche-quoting counterpart to Batman — the value of the battery industry will increase from €15 ($18) billion in Europe and €75 ($90) billion worldwide in 2019 to €35 ($42) billion in Europe and €130 ($156) billion worldwide by 2030.</li> \n</ul> \n<p>So, who will be Europe's answer to CATL (short for Contemporary Amperex Technology Co. Ltd.), China's main battery manufacturer? There are several pretenders to the crown. Here are some:</p> \n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"> \n <li>Britishvolt, set to go online with Britain's first and largest gigafactory in Northumberland (UK) in 2023, with a maximum capacity of 35 GWh per annum. </li> \n <li>Northvolt, led by former Tesla execs, supported by the Swedish government and the European Investment Bank. Also funded by Volkswagen and Goldman Sachs. Aims to be green and big. One plant coming online in Sweden this year, another in Germany in 2024. Combined maximum capacity is 64 GWh. </li> \n <li>Tesla. Not content with its one gigafactory (40 GWh) opening this year, the company has already announced that it will build a second plant in Europe.</li> \n</ul> \n<p>That second plant is not yet on the map. Also missing are the half dozen gigafactories that Volkswagen aims to open in the coming years. If Europe is to become self-sufficient in EV batteries, even more will be needed.</p> \n<p><strong>Europe's path to battery supremacy</strong></p> \n<p>In 2020, 1.3 million EVs were sold in Europe, edging past China to become the world's largest EV market. In 2021, Europe looks set to maintain that lead. By 2025 at the latest, EVs <a href=\"https://about.bnef.com/blog/electric-cars-reach-price-parity-2025/\" target=\"_blank\">will have achieved price parity</a> with fossil-fuel vehicles, not just in terms of total cost of operation but also in upfront cost.</p> \n<p>Add to that the increasingly hostile environment — namely, higher taxes and stricter regulations — to fossil-fuel cars in Europe, and the pace of electrification will increase dramatically by mid-decade. Going by EU requirements for CO2 emissions alone, the EV share of the total vehicle market would need to be between 60% and 70% pretty soon.</p> \n<p>While that may seem an impossibly high target today, things could start looking different very soon. Volkswagen aims to have full-electric cars make up <a href=\"https://europe.autonews.com/automakers/vw-brand-will-accelerate-electric-vehicle-shift\" target=\"_blank\">more than 70 percent of its European sales</a> by 2030. Volvo and Ford even aim to present entirely electric lineups by 2030 at the latest. And that year is also when the UK government intends to <a href=\"https://www.forbes.com/sites/neilwinton/2020/11/18/britains-accelerated-ice-vehicle-sales-ban-aims-to-speed-co2-progress-with-unintended-consequences/?sh=465906421ffa\" target=\"_blank\">ban the sale</a> of new fossil-fuel cars.</p> \n<p>All of which could translate into base demand for EV batteries in Europe as high as 1,200 GWh by 2040. Even with all planned factories on the map running at maximum capacity, that still leaves a production capacity gap of about 40%.</p> \n<p>To avoid batteries becoming a bottleneck for electrification, the EU likely will pour even more money into the industry via the <a href=\"https://ec.europa.eu/info/strategy/priorities-2019-2024/european-green-deal_en\" target=\"_blank\">European Green Deal</a> and Europe's post-COVID recovery plan. Battery production is not just strategically sound; it also boosts employment.</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://deinenergieportal.de/?p=12892\" target=\"_blank\">A study by Fraunhofer ISI</a> says for each GWh added in battery production capacity, count on 40 jobs added directly and 200 in upstream industries. The study forecasts battery manufacturing could generate up to 155,000 jobs across Europe by 2033 (although it doesn't mention how many would be lost due to reduced production of fossil-fuel cars).</p> \n<p><strong>Coming to America</strong></p> \n<p>And how fares America? Electrification is coming to the U.S. as well. By <a href=\"https://cleantechnica.com/2021/04/20/lithium-ev-battery-forecasts-for-europe-us-china-2025-2030-part-1/\" target=\"_blank\">one estimate</a>, EVs will have a market penetration of about 15% by 2025. Deloitte <a href=\"https://cleantechnica.com/2021/04/20/lithium-ev-battery-forecasts-for-europe-us-china-2025-2030-part-1/\" target=\"_blank\">predicts</a> EVs will take up 27% of new car sales in the US by 2030. The Biden administration is keen to make up for past inaction in terms of switching to post-fossil energy. But it has its work cut out.</p> \n<p>Apart from Tesla's Gigafactory, the U.S. has only two other battery production facilities. If current trends continue, there would be just ten by 2030. At that time, China will have 140 battery factories and Europe, according to this map, close to 30. If U.S. production can't keep up with demand, electrification will suffer from the dreaded battery bottleneck. Unless America is content to import its batteries from Europe or China.</p> \n<p><br /><em>This map was produced by <a href=\"https://cicenergigune.com/en\" target=\"_blank\">CIC energiGUNE</a>, a research center for electrochemical and thermal energy storage, set up by the <a href=\"https://www.euskadi.eus/eusko-jaurlaritza/hasiera/\" target=\"_blank\">government of the Basque Country</a>. Image found <a href=\"https://twitter.com/energigune_brta/status/1382637523948683264/photo/1\" target=\"_blank\">here</a> on their <a href=\"https://twitter.com/energigune_brta\" target=\"_blank\">Twitter</a>.</em></p> \n<p><strong>Strange Maps #1080</strong></p> \n<p><strong></strong><em>Got a strange map? Let me know at <a href=\"mailto:strangemaps@gmail.com\">strangemaps@gmail.com</a>.</em></p> \n<p><em> Follow Strange Maps on <a href=\"https://twitter.com/VeryStrangeMaps\" target=\"_blank\">Twitter</a> and <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/VeryStrangeMaps\" target=\"_blank\">Facebook</a>. </em><br /></p> \n<div></div> \n<ul class=\"ee-ul\"></ul> \n<div></div> \n<img
